execution of an acceptable financial agency agreement between SP and a bank for XRBSL
processing (Annex 12) are conditions of disbursement for the loan proceeds allocated to the
Environmental Pollution Prevention and Control component.
H. LAND ACQUISITION AND RESETTLEMENT
3.25  The project has four components that entail land acquisition and resettlement:
(a) the Jinan Yellow River Water Supply Project; (b) the Jinan Waste Water Collection
Project; (c) the Yantai Municipal District Heating Project; and (d) the Weihai Municipal
District Heating Project. The first subcomponent is entirely rural resettlement while the
other three subcomponent involve mainly urban resettlement. The total amount of
permanent land acquisition is 992 hectares. Compensation is also to be given for another
300 hectares of land that would be temporarily impacted after 2015. The number of
people to be physically relocated is 2,120 (667 households). The number of people who
require new livelihoods is 5,097, including dependents. The total number of people who
are directly affected by the project and require compensation is 6,031. The Jinan Yellow
River Water Supply Project requires the largest scale of resettlement and rehabilitation
(4,450 people).
3.26 Resettlement Action Plans (RAPs). In compliance with Operational Directive
4.30, each subcomponent has prepared a RAP, all of which have been reviewed by the
Mission's resettlement expert and found to be generally in accordance with the
requirements of the Bank. Each RAP sets out: (a) land acquisition and resettlement
policies and principles; (b) baseline surveys; (c) description of resettlement sites;
(d) resettlement  implementation  arrangements;  (e)  implementation  schedule;
(f) rehabilitation of livelihoods; (g) costs and budget; (h) participation of project-affected
people (PAP); (i) grievance mechanisms: and (j) monitoring and evaluation of RAP
implementation.
3.27 Rural Resettlement (Jinan Yellow River Water Supply Project). For the 304
households that require physical relocation, two-thirds would be provided new residential
sites nearby while the remainder (all from one village) would be relocated collectively to



- 27 -
a rural area within 5 km of the original location. The final location of housing would be
determined by the affected households. Most have expressed agreement to move to the
designated areas because the conditions would be better than the existing situations,
which suffer for poor drainage and related health problems. Compensation for housing
and other personal assets would be assessed and paid at the full replacement value,
without any allowance being deducted for depreciation. For the 4,450 people who lose
most of their agricultural land, compensation would be provided in the form of new land,
sharing of improved farmland, development of land-intensive agricultural activities (i.e.,
animal husbandry, fish ponds, vegetables), agricultural sideline activities, or job creation
schemes. Since the affected area is located in the suburbs of Jinan City, access to
employment activities is very good. The Jinan Municipal Government has approved a
project-specific regulation for land requisition and resettlement, and has proposed
preferential policies to reduce grain quotas, agricultural taxes, and education fees for the
project-affected people. JWSC has agreed to provide some construction jobs to the
affected persons and provide technical assistance for the construction of piped water
supply schemes for the affected villages.
3.28  Urban Resettlement. For the Jinan Waster Water Collection Project, 1,303
people are directly affected and entitled to compensation (including 475 people for the
retroactive component). The 363 households that require physical relocation can choose
between new government housing or cash compensation to relocate on their own. Most
of the affected households currently rent their housing, so they typically prefer to move to
new government housing and continue to rent. The new rental housing generally
provides better living conditions. For people who own their houses, they would be paid
the full replacement value, without any allowance being deducted for depreciation.
Allowances would be provided for moving expenses and lost wages during the transition
period. Livelihoods for people to be relocated would not be affected beyond the
transition phase. There are 82 commercial shops to be relocated and 21 enterprises or
institutions that are partially affected. The owners would be compensated for lost assets,
income and wages and would receive allowances during the transition phase. Any
workers affected by the relocation of shops or the disruption of enterprises would be
compensated for lost wages and guaranteed continued employment or assistance in
securing new employment. The first phase of the project has already involved the
resettlement of 119 households, 29 commercial shops and 7 enterprises. A separate RAP
for retroactive approval has been reviewed by the World Bank. Monitoring of the
completed resettlement was conducted by an independent agency (Shandong University)
and was reported to have been implemented satisfactorily.
3.29 For the Weihai District Heating component, the municipal government acquired
16 hectares of land from villagers in 1993.  The 56 affected farmers have been
compensated and employed in rural enterprises. WBHPC has received approval to
purchase the land from the municipal government as the site for the Weihai No. 3 Heat
and Power Plant. WBHPC prepared a retroactive RAP, which has been reviewed by the
World Bank. An independent agency (Shandong Academy of Social Sciences) has
reported satisfactory completion of resettlement with substantial increase in incomes of



- 28 -
all affected persons. Since 1993, two enterprises have located on the new site without
permits. WBHPC has agreed to compensate both enterprises for the full replacement
value of assets, and would assist the enterprises to find new land to reconstruct their
facilities. Once new facilities are constructed, the enterprises can be easily shifted with
no loss of revenue.
3.30  The Yantai District Heating component affects 117 people and 4.3 hectares of
land. No households are required to be physically relocated. Compensation for housing
and other personal assets would be assessed and paid at the full replacement value,
without any allowance being deducted for depreciation. Land acquisition would affect
one brick factory, which would be compensated and the investment would be used to start
a new production facility that can employ all 112 affected staff.
3.31  Resettlement Costs. The cost of fully implementing each RAP has been included
in the base cost for the Shandong Environment Project. The total cost of implementing
the four RAPs is estimated at Y 252 million (January 1997 prices), including physical and
price contingencies. Land acquisition and resettlement represents 14 percent of the total
project cost.
3.32 Implementation Arrangements. Resettlement would be implemented largely by
the project agencies, particularly for the urban components. Local government would
also play a key role to ensure the implementation is smooth and the compensation
amounts are acceptable to the project-affected persons. For the Jinan Wastewater
component, the Urban and Rural Construction Bureau would ensure that adequate
government housing options are available. For the Jinan Yellow River Water Supply
component, the local District and Town governments, along with the Jinan Land
Administration Bureau, would ensure the land compensation is adequate and that host
land acquisition and land readjustments are carried out: (a) according to Chinese laws
and local regulations; and (b) taking full consideration of the preferences of the affected
villages.
3.33  SPPMO is responsible for coordination of resettlement issues that require
interagency actions/decisions and for liaison with the World Bank. SPPMO would
ensure that each of the implementing agencies engages the services of independent
monitoring agencies (e.g., the Shandong Academy of Social Sciences or Shandong
University) as external resettlement monitors.
3.34 Implementation of the RAPs would be carried out in consultation with, and with
the participation of, the affected persons and their representatives. Mechanisms for the
redress of grievances of affected persons and adequate avenues for appeal exist.
3.35 Monitoring and Evaluation. The implementation of each RAP would have both
internal and external monitoring and evaluation. Internal monitoring would be carried
out by each project implementing agency, and quarterly reports would be provided to
SPPMO. The internal monitoring report formats have been agreed with the World Bank.
Resettlement evaluation criteria and monitoring indicators have been specified for each



- 29 -
subcomponent. The external monitors would verify the status of implementation as
reported in the internal reports and would conduct independent surveys to assess the
socioeconomic and qualitative aspects of resettlement.  For the Jinan Wastewater
Collection Project, the first external monitoring reports would be due March 1997, and
then semiannually thereafter. The first report for the Jinan Yellow River Water Supply
Project would be due June 1997, and then provided semiannually. For Yantai and Weihai
subprojects, only one or two external monitoring and evaluation reports is required
because resettlement is relatively minor and the implementation would be completed by
the end of 1997.
3.36 The framework for land acquisition and resettlement is shown in Annex 6.
During negotiations, assurances were obtained from Shandong Province and the local
municipal governments that they would: (a) carry out the resettlement and rehabilitation
of affected persons in a manner, and according to the RAPs, that is consistent with World
Bank guidelines; and (b) report quarterly on the progress using reporting formats agreed
to with the World Bank
I. ENVIRONMENTAL IMPACT ASSESSMENT
3.37  The proposed project is classified as Category A for purposes of Environmental
Assessment (EA). In accordance with Chinese regulations, component-specific EAs and
a comprehensive project-wide EA were prepared. The project-wide EA has been
reviewed by the National Environmental Protection Agency (NEPA) and the Bank and
found to be satisfactory. The findings of the EA are summarized in Annex 7. To ensure
satisfactory implementation of EA findings, SEPB has prepared an implementation
program. The environmental impact of the project is, on balance, substantially positive
and the benefits greatly outweigh the negative impacts. The project would reduce
pollution loads to the Xiaoqing River from Jinan, substantially reduce the present
unsustainable levels of groundwater abstractions in Jinan, and reduce coal consumption
and polluting emissions in both Weihai and Yantai.
3.38  There are, however, some negative impacts that require mitigation. Appropriate
arrangements have been made for the beneficial use, wherever possible, of construction
spoil sludge from wastewater treatment in Jinan and residues from coal combustion in
Weihai and Yantai. During negotiations, assurances were obtainedfrom SP that it would
carry out or would cause to be carried out, in a manner satisfactory to the Bank; the
findings of the EA and implementation program.
J. PROJECT REPORTING AND SUPERVISION
3.39 The proposed project implementation schedule is shown in Annex 8. During
negotiations, assurances were obtained that reports on project progress, including
progress on the implementation program of the EA, based on agreed monitoring indicators,
would be prepared by each implementing agency, consolidated by SPPMO, andforwarded
to the Bank by February 28, April 30, July 31 and October 31 of each year, commencing in
February 1998. The last progress report of each year would include the latest project cost



- 30 -
estimate and financing plan for the following year, including SP 's draft budget for local
counterpart funds.  A  list of proposed monitoring indicators for environmental
improvement is included as Annex 9. An implementation completion report, reviewing
the planned objectives and the achievements of the project, including costs and benefits
derived, and performance and contribution of all parties associated with project execution,
would be prepared by SP and submitted to the Bank within six months of the Closing Date.
3.40 To facilitate implementation, a Project Launch Workshop would be held in late
1997. Project supervision would monitor impact with respect to the objectives (para. 3.5),
and focus on key sector issues such as utility pricing and environmental regulatory
effectiveness. Bank Group experience with supervision of the ongoing Urban Infrastructure
and Environment Project in Liaoning Province, the Hubei Urban Environmental Project and
the Yunnan Environment Project, which, like the proposed project, are multicity projects,
suggests that Bank supervision would require about 105 staff-weeks over the life of the
project, of which about 35 staff-weeks would be at headquarters for the review of progress
and audit reports, procurement actions and correspondence, etc., and 70 staff-weeks would
be in the field. More intensive supervision would be required during the first three years of
the project, during which time the technical assistance would be made operational and many
procurement contracts would have been tendered, evaluated and awarded. Supervision
would be provided by environmental engineering and financial staff, as well as other
specialists, as needed. The Project Supervision Plan is shown in Annex 10.
3.41  A mid-term review would be held in about mid-2000 to assess critically the progress
in implementation of the various components of the project. The mid-term review mission
would pay particular attention to utility tariffs, environmental regulation, and associated
policy issues critical to project and sector success. During negotiations, assurances were
obtained that a mid-term review ofproject progress would be undertaken by September 30,
2000, and the agreed recommendations implemented thereafter.



- 31 -
4. PROJECT MANAGEMENT AND IMPLEMENTATION
A. PROJECT MANAGEMENT
4.1   A Leading Group, headed by a vice-governor, was set up to guide and give policy
direction for the preparation, and subsequently, implementation of the proposed
Shandong Environment Project. Members of the Group include the Vice-Secretary
General of the Shandong Provincial Government (SPG), and directors from the Shandong
Provincial Planning Commission, Shandong Provincial Urban and Rural Construction
Commission, Shandong Finance Bureau (SFB) and Shandong Environmental Protection
Bureau (SEPB). The Leading Group designated the Construction Commission as the
agency responsible for managing the project as the planning, construction and
management of environmental infrastructure is the responsibility of construction
commissions within China. The Construction Commission in turn established the
Shandong Provincial Project Management Office (SPPMO) to prepare and execute the
project, including the supervision and monitoring of contracts, maintenance of project
records, and the submission of progress reports to the Bank (para. 3.39). SPPMO is
headed by a director who is assisted by four deputy directors from the Shandong
Provincial Planning Commission, Shandong Provincial Urban and Rural Construction
Commission, SFB and SEPB. Leading Groups were also set up in each of the project
municipalities of Jinan, Weihai and Yantai, under which municipal project offices were
established within the municipalities' urban and rural construction commissions. During
project preparation, SPPMO and the municipal project offices received regular policy
guidance from their respective leading groups. The municipal project offices in turn
created and staffed subproject offices in each of the implementing agencies listed in Table
4.1 below.
4.2   SPPMO, working closely with SEPB and other interested government agencies, has
provided functional guidance to the municipal project offices, has interacted well with them
and with its consultants and design institutes, has expedited resources to undertake
preparation, and has arranged for the project preparation work to be completed to the
satisfaction of SP and the Bank. SPPMO would have the following responsibilities during
project implementation:
(a)   overall project coordination, macro-level project management and
monitoring;
(b)   annual budget preparation;
(c)   projectwide quality assurance;



- 32 -
(d)   progress reporting to SP and the Bank, including cost management, project
impact and environmental improvement assessment;
(e)   interagency coordination and procurement support; and
(f)   sectoral training facilitation.
4.3    To enable SPPMO to undertake this work effectively, it has been strengthened, and
the personnel skill mix adjusted to reflect the needs of the implementation and construction
phase of the project. During negotiations, assurances were obtainedfrom SP that SPPMO
and the participating municipalities' project offices would be maintained throughout
implementation with functions and responsibilities satisfactory to the Bank, with competent
staff in adequate numbers for the duration of the project.
B. IMPLEMENTATION RESPONSIBILITIES
4.4    The institutions designated by SP and its municipal governments to implement, and
finance, the various components of the project are listed in Table 4.1.

4.5    Provincial and Municipal Institutions.  Shandong Province is headed by a
governor who is assisted by eight vice-governors, who oversee several commissions
and/or departments. Various provincial commissions, departments and bureaus provide
supervision and guidance to the municipalities on matters relating to infrastructure,
planning, development and operation, including environmental protection works.
Organization charts of SPG, the project municipalities, and the project implementing
agencies, including their interrelationship, are given in Annex 11.
4.6    The organization of the municipal governments of Jinan, Weihai and Yantai is
similar. Municipal affairs are overseen by the municipal economic and planning



- 33 -
commissions, and municipal urban and rural construction commissions.  The latter
commissions supervise a number of bureaus that are responsible for the physical planning,
design, construction, operation and maintenance of urban infrastructure, including
environmental infrastructure, and for environmental protection. In addition, several bureaus
concerned with urban infrastructure report directly to the office of the mayor. These include
the Finance Bureau, the Water Conservancy Bureau, the Bureau of Public Health and
Public Security Bureau.
4.7   As part of China's and Shandong's policy of reforms in the public utilities sector,
the participating municipalities recently established self-accounting and self-funding state-
owned enterprises to implement the wastewater component (Jinan) and the district heating
component (Weihai and Yantai) (paras. 4.10-4.12). The water supply and district heating
components of Jinan would be implemented by the municipality's existing water supply
and district heating agencies, established earlier. Prior to negotiations, the Bank received
(a) conformed copies of the charters, and (b) business licenses of all these enterprises.
4.8   Water Supply. The Jinan Water Supply Company (JWSC) was established in
1936 as a self-funding, self-accounting, state-owned enterprise, responsible for the
planning, construction management, operation and maintenance, consumer service
(billing and collection), and administration of water supply in Jinan City. JWSC reports
to the Jinan Municipal Affairs Management Bureau, which comes under the Jinan Urban
and Rural Construction Commission. At the time of its establishment, it supplied about
7,600 m3/day for about 1,700 residential and industrial units in the commercial area of the
city. Currently, its installed design capacity is around 895,000 m3/day. However, actual
available supply is some 714,000 m3/day owing to resource constraints. Like other long-
established water supply companies in China, JWSC has built up a solid reputation for
providing a reliable and good-quality water supply. However, with total personnel of
about 2,950, staffing levels and its administration costs are high by industry standards.
Technical assistance to be provided under the project would help JWSC to rationalize its
expenses and improve its accounting and management information systems.
4.9   Wastewater. Prior to the creation of the wastewater entity in Jinan (para. 4.10),
wastewater collection, treatment and disposal were provided by the Jinan Drainage and
Administration Division (JDAD), which reported to the Jinan Urban Construction Bureau.
JDAD did not operate on a financially autonomous basis, transferring, instead, all revenues
collected to its parent bureau. Funds were consolidated at that level and reallocated for
operation, maintenance and capital works, on the basis of budgets prepared and submitted
by JDAD.
4.10 The Jinan Municipal Drainage Company (JMDC) was established in September
1996 as a self-funding, self-accounting, state-owned enterprise, to be responsible for the
planning, construction management, operation and maintenance, consumer service
(billing and collection), and administration of wastewater collection, treatment and
disposal in Jinan City. It has a sanctioned staff strength of 1,218, of which about 1,023
were transferred from JDAD and 173 from the sewerage treatment works of Jinan Urban



- 34 -
and Rural Construction Commission. While the staff are familiar with wastewater
operations, there is as yet no corporate tradition in JMDC. Technical assistance to be
provided under the project would assist JMDC to implement its organizational plans, and
establish accounting, finance and management information systems appropriate for a
large public utility. Existing wastewater assets, in the form of sewers, pump stations,
wastewater treatment plant and monitoring station, have been transferred to JMDC.
While JMDC would account for its own capital and operating costs, it would, for the
present, be required to remit its revenues to its parent bureau, such funds being eannarked
to finance the operation and maintenance of the sewerage facilities, and service debt. The
remitted revenues, therefore, would be returned in their entirety to JMDC. While less
desirable than full autonomy, some water companies function efficiently under such a
system. JWSC, however, retains its revenues to finance its operations.
District Heating Companies
4.11 Weihai Botong Heat and Power Company Ltd. The Weihai Botong Heat and
Power Company Ltd. (WBHPC) was established in September 1996 as a self-funding,
self-accounting, state-owned enterprise, to be responsible for the planning, construction
management, operation and maintenance, consumer service (billing and collection), and
administration of the proposed district heating investments to serve the southern part of
Weihai City, comprising about 24 km2. It reports to the Weihai Municipal Affairs
Management Bureau, which comes under the Weihai Urban and Rural Construction
Commission. When fully operational, WBHPC would have a staff strength of about 375,
and a production capacity of 24 MW of electricity, 60 MW of hot water, and 75 tons/hour
of steam.
4.12 Yantai. The Yantai Heat Supply Company Ltd. (YHSC) was established in
September 1996 as a self-funding, self-accounting, state-owned enterprise, to be
responsible for the planning, construction management, operation and maintenance,
consumer service (billing and collection), and administration of the proposed district
heating investments to serve the central urban, and densely populated, area (Zhifu
District) of Yantai. YHSC would purchase heat from an existing power company, the
Huang Bo-Hai Power Plant Company Ltd., for distribution to its customers. YHSC has
entered into an agreement with Huang Bo-Hai Power Plant Company Ltd. for the
purchase of bulk heat supplies. The agreement stipulates the terms and conditions of
water and electricity sales, as well as the boundary limits of hardware and heat supply
control principles. A copy of the agreement is in the project file (Annex 16). YHSC
reports to the Yantai Municipal Affairs Management Bureau, which comes under the
Yantai Urban and Rural Construction Commission. When fully operational, YHSC
would have a staff strength of about 545, and a connected heat load of about 500 MW.
4.13 Both Weihai and Yantai (para. 4.12) cities had had previous experience in
operating district heating systems so some staff are partially trained. Technical assistance
to be provided under the project would, however, assist the cities to establish their newly-



- 35 -
formed companies on modem utility lines, supplemented by corresponding accounting
and management information systems.
4.14 Environmental Pollution Prevention and ControL The Xiaoqing River Basin
Subloans Facility (XRBSL) would be operated by SEPB in collaboration with SFB, with
segregated accounting and special operating procedures. The management and operation of
XRBSL are summarized in Annex 12. The purpose of the subloans facility would be to
provide funds for environmental pollution prevention and control, targeted to small- and
medium-size enterprises located in the XRB. Project selection, appraisal, both technical
and financial, and environmental criteria have been proposed and generally follow those of
similar funds in operation elsewhere in China. Projects would be selected according to cost
effectiveness and financial viability, with the first three appraisals and all appraisals for
proposed subloans of over $300,000 receiving ex-ante review by the Bank and the
remainder subject to ex-post review. The principal funding sources for XRBSL would be
funds allocated by SP (para. 3.14), the proceeds of the Bank loan, and recycling of loan and
interest payments.
4.15  The environmental degradation of the XRB is of sufficient gravity to motivate
SPG to an prepare environmental action plan to address the most significant pollution
issues in the river basin, together with working-level plans for the three top-ranked point-
source polluters. During negotiations, assurances were obtained from SP that it would
carry out, or cause to be carried out: (a) its time-bound XRB Environmental Action
Plan, acceptable to the Bank, to address the significant point sources ofpollution in that
area (including implementing measures to strengthen the pollution discharge permit
system); (b) an environmental data collection update, to be completed by June 30, 1998,
and which, on completion, would be reviewed with the Bank; and (c) time-bound
Environmental Action Plans for the three top-ranked polluters in the XRB. These latter
include two paper mills and one brewery. SPG is also proposing to carry out a study
designed to assist its XRB Commission, SEPB and other responsible agencies, to
implement recently issued provincial government pollution control decisions and
regulations (para. 2.24), including river section pollution control allocation and discharge
pernit system. Arrangements for the safe disposal of power plant residues in Weihai and
Yantai were reviewed at appraisal, and satisfactory arrangements were identified for this
disposal.
4.16 Technical Assistance and Training. As a wastewater company in Jinan and the
district heating companies of Weihai and Yantai have been created only recently, the
project's management and agency strengthening component has been designed specifically
to address the accounting, management information, financial management, utility pricing,
technical requirements and training needs of these implementing entities. Some technical
assistance would also be provided SPPMO, and to the existing JWSC to strengthen its
management capability.

5. FINANCE
A. FINANCIAL MANAGEMENT
5.1   Since 1978, Chinese economic reforms have evolved gradually toward an
increasingly decentralized financial system in China. Most industrial production and
prices have become free of government control, and enterprises have to purchase most of
their inputs at market prices. Enterprise reform has become a high priority of the
Government. In this connection, the guiding principles of the recently promulgated
"regulations on transforming the management mechanisms of state-owned industrial
enterprises" are to separate government administration from enterprise management,
increase the autonomy and accountability of state-owned enterprises, and increase
competition and market orientation. The reform efforts to improve economic policies and
enterprise efficiencies are essential for sustaining both economic development and
environmental protection in the longer run.
5.2   In order for the enterprises to assume their increased responsibilities efficiently and
effectively, the strengthening of their financial management is essential. Toward this end,
the proposed project would support the applications of modern management techniques and
huuman resource development for the utility companies.
B. ACCOUNTING
5.3   Under the project, the following accounting systems, in accordance with
regulations prescribed by the Ministry of Finance, apply.
5.4   Enterprise Accounting. All state-owned enterprises in China follow a unified
enterprise accounting system. The accounting framework was developed in the context of a
highly centralized planned economy. With a view to complementing the ongoing efforts to
deepen reforms in the financial sector and enterprise management, the accounting system in
China needed modernization to permit satisfactory financial management in an increasingly
decentralized and market-oriented environment. As a first step, the general principles of
enterprise (the term to include public utilities such as water, sewerage, and district heating
companies) accounting were revised by the Ministry of Finance from July 1, 1993 to be
more consistent with internationally generally accepted accounting practice. Further
reforms are under way to combine aspects of the Constructioii Unit Accounting System
with the Enterprise Accounting System for state-owned enterprises that are responsible for
construction of their own investment projects. Until the Government has completed the
modifications, all enterprises included in the project would follow both systems.
5.5   Government Accounting. The regular government department accounts reflect
expenditures incurred against budget allocations by SP or its municipal governments.



- 38 -
Accounting is handled in a decentralized manner through transfers to subunits from which
actual expenditures are made and recorded on a cash basis. Accounts are kept at all levels,
and each subunit reflects in its accounts the budget allocation it receives from the unit
above.
5.6   Project Accounts.  Project accounts would be maintained by SPPMO, in
accordance with the State Construction Unit Accounting System. This system prescribes
generally accepted accounting practices, including double-entry bookkeeping, subsidiary
ledger accounting, and rules for capitalizing and expending various items of expenditure. It
also sets out detailed accounting definitions for capital works, cash control, receivables and
payables. In addition, it identifies a number of basic monthly, quarterly and annual
reporting formats, including funds flow statements and balance sheets. The construction
accounting system is comprehensive, and is adequate for the proposed project.
C. AUDITS
5.7   As with other Bank-financed projects in China, the Foreign Investment Audit
Bureau of the China National Audit Office (CNAO), established in 1983 under the name of
State Audit Administration, would have overall responsibility for auditing accounts
concerning the project. The actual auditing work would be conducted by the Shandong
Provincial Audit Department under CNAO's supervision. This arrangement is satisfactory.
During negotiations, assurances were obtained that the following annual audits would be
submitted to the Bank within six months after the end of the financial year: (a) audit of the
project accounts maintained by SPPMO, (b) audit of the Special Account; (c) audit of
SOEs; and (d) audits of the financial statements of (i) JWSC, (ii) JMDC, (iii) WBHPC,
(iv) YHSC; and (v) XRBSL. Understandings were obtained during negotiations that audit
reports would include opinions on whether implementing agencies were in compliance
with their respective financial covenants, and whether these agencies had taken out
adequate insurance on goods and works financed from loan proceeds. The Bank would
expect to receive the first such audit reports by June 30, 1998, covering project
implementation activities commencing in fiscal year 1997.
D. PROVINCIAL FINANCE
5.8   SPG has two types of finance, on-budgetary and off-budgetary. On-budgetary
revenues are provided through taxes, the base and rate of which are set by the Central
Government. The Central Government directly controls the single largest tax source-the
value-added tax-receipts from which are shared with local government. A variety of other
taxes are collected and used directly by local government. Most off-budgetary revenues
consist of special fees paid by enterprises, and fees and charges relating to construction
activities. These are used for capital expenditures. On-budgetary and off-budgetary
accounts are not consolidated. Information on off-budgetary revenues and expenditures is
not formally published; the appraisal mission was satisfied that these are at least as large as
the on-budgetary data, thereby providing an assurance that provincial resources are
adequate to support project implementation.



- 39 -
5.9   SP would recover the debt associated with the project as it is being passed on to the
municipalities and industrial enterprises. SP would monitor municipal finances to assure
that the municipalities and their respective agencies meet their debt service obligations.
Should the municipalities and their subborrowers be unable to fulfill their obligations, SP
would have no difficulty in covering debt service through its own resources, as the debt
service represented by the project is small compared to total provincial budgetary
expenditures. Provincial fiscal budgetary revenues and expenditures totaled about Y 38.9
billion ($4.7 billion) and Y 34.3 billion ($4.1 billion), respectively, in 1995. Between 1993
and 1995, revenues and expenditures increased on average by about 20 and 18 percent a
year in current terms, respectively. Revenues are derived mainly from taxes on industry and
commerce, which have been remitted by the municipalities. Revenues and expenditures are
projected to grow at about 10 percent per year in current terms, equivalent to between about
2 and 4 percent a year in real terms, during the project period. Projected receipts and
expenditures for Shandong Province and the Municipalities of Jinan, Weihai and Yantai are
provided in the report on Provincial and Municipal Financial Projections for fiscal years
1993 to 2005 (on-budget), which is included in the Project File (Annext16).
E. MUNICIPAL FINANCE
5.10  SP would, through the Municipalities of Jinan, Weihai and Yantai, provide all
funding not met by (a) the proceeds of the Bank loan, (b) municipal contributions, and (c)
funds generated internally by the implementing agencies. During project implementation,
the financial projections show that these requirements would be of the order of Y 544
million for Jinan, Y 232 million for Weihai, and Y 206 million for Yantai. The
Municipalities, like SP, have two sources of finance, on-budgetary and off-budgetary. The
off-budget details are confidential, and were not, therefore, provided to the Bank. The
municipal funding requirements would come from many more sources than municipal on-
budget revenue. SFB has independently assessed the municipalities' ability to generate the
required counterpart funds from assured sources.
5.11   Summarized projected on-budget revenue and expenditure statements of the
municipalities of Jinan, Weihai and Yantai for the period 1996-2003 are given in
Annex 13, together with assumptions to the projections. Average annual project-related
expenditures comprise between about 0.1 to 9.8 percent of the municipalities' annual total
on-budget expenditures.
F. TARIFFS
5.12  The water and wastewater tariffs are categorized into residential, commercial,
industrial and institutional consumers. The water tariff comprises three parts, a fee to
cover operation and maintenance costs, a water supply fund fee to finance system
capacity expansion, and a public utility tax. The wastewater tariff is charged against 80
percent of water consumption, the assumed wastewater discharge level, and is set at 30
percent of the water tariff. The wastewater fees are billed by the water supply company
on behalf of the wastewater company and transferred from JWSC to JMDC through the
Jinan Finance Bureau on a quarterly basis. Separately, the Water Conservation Office



- 40 -
collects a wastewater fee from industrial users based on groundwater reserve quotas and
remits this revenue to the wastewater company.
5.13 In May 1996, the Jinan Municipal Government (JMG) authorized increases in the
water supply tariffs with the result that both the JWSC and JMDC would-be able to meet
their financial covenants (paras. 5.21 and 5.22, respectively) in fiscal year 1997. The
current average water supply tariff is Y 0.70/m3; the current average wastewater tariff is
Y 0.33/m 3, based on 80 percent of water consumption.
5.14 Weihai would construct a combined heat and power (CHP) plant under the project
to offer district heating in winter, and cooling in summer to residential, commercial, and
institutional consumers, and sell steam to industry consumers year-round. Power
produced by the CHPs would be sold to the local grids at prevailing prices. WBHPC
expects to begin selling steam and heat commencing in 1998 when prices would need to
be set at Y 36.80/GJ and Y 23.00/m2, respectively. YHSC is primarily a distribution
company purchasing heat from an existing CHP, but it would also construct and operate
peak boilers to supply district heating to residential, institutional and commercial
customers. YHSC expects to sell district heating in limited quantities commencing in
1997. The average local heat tariff, which was raised on January 1, 1997 from
Y 18.72/n2 to Y 23.53/mi, is sufficient to enable YHSC to meet its financial covenant in
fiscal year 1997 (see Table 5.1).
5.15   The district heating and cooling tariffs consist of one charge only, based on the
area to be serviced. The steam sales tariff also has only one component, an energy
charge. In Weihai and Yantai, district heating bills of residential consumers are paid by
their sponsoring unit as part of their work benefits.

5.16 The financial objectives set by the municipalities of Jinan, Weihai and Yantai for
their water, wastewater and district heating companies are for the agencies to achieve full
cost recovery on their operations, and in the case of JWSC, to make a reasonable



-41 -
contribution to its capital investment program as well. The annual tariff adjustments
required to meet these financial objectives are shown in Tables 5.2 and 5.3, and in Annex
14. However, in practice, water, wastewater and district heating tariffs are adjusted only
every two to three years at a time. It is possible, therefore, that in the intervening years
between tariff adjustments, some or all of the agencies may not be able to meet their
annual targets. In such eventualities, the agencies' operating revenues would need to be
supplemented from the general revenues of the parent municipality.
G. WATER SUPPLY AND WASTEWATER AGENCIES
5.17 Jinan Water Supply Company (JWSC). JWSC has in recent years relied on
government operating subsidies to maintain its operations. In 1996, its net losses are
expected to reduce to about 2 percent of sales revenue due to its mid-year implementation
of increased domestic, commercial and industrial tariffs. It had previously increased its
industry tariff effective the end of 1995. Electricity and administration are JWSC's
primary costs. Electricity is required to operate pumping stations. Administration, which
includes pensions, health care costs, and management salaries, would account for 20
percent of revenues in 1996, and is considered high by industry standards. JWSC plans
to limit increases in its administration budget; technical assistance proposed under the
project would assist JWSC to rationalize its expenses, including administrative expenses.
The financial objectives set for JWSC by JMG are for it to make reasonable contributions
to its capital investment program, after meeting its cash expenses. Projections made up to
the year 2003 show that JWSC would post surpluses if it is permitted to periodically
adjust tariffs as shown in Table 5.2 and improve operating efficiency by limiting
additional increases in management and meter reading staff.
5.18  JWSC's largest cost is electricity, amounting to 35 percent of its operating
revenues. About 58 percent of JWSC's revenues are derived from industrial consumers,
about 36 percent from residential consumers, and 6 percent from commercial consumers,
a trend which is expected to continue in the future. Billing and collections operate
smoothly, with collections running less than one month on average. During the project
implementation period from 1997 to 2003, JWSC's Y 1.4 billion capital investment
program, and changes in working capital and cash are expected to be financed 29 percent
from internal sources, 37 percent from long-term borrowing, and 34 percent from
govermment counterpart funds. Corporate debt is expected to remain below 20 percent
during the implementation period, which would enable further borrowing for future
expansion.
5.19 Jinan Municipal Drainage Company (JMDC). Prior to the formation of JMDC
as a public enterprise on September 10, 1996, the Jinan Public Utilities Bureau, Drainage
Division, oversaw sewerage collection, treatment and disposal, and the Jinan Municipal
Urban Construction Bureau managed the planning and construction of new wastewater
assets. The total value of the wastewater treatment works, completed in 1995, and
drainage network assets transferred to JMDC, is Y 343 million. The financial objective



- 42 -
set by JMG for JMDC is to achieve full cost recovery, and if possible, generate some
funds for future capital investment.

5.20   Since wastewater revenues are directly proportional to water sales, industry,
followed by residential consumers, are the largest revenue sources of JMDC. The largest
cash expense is electricity, accounting for 65 percent of expenses. JMDC's capital
investment program, increases in working capital and cash changes, totaling about Y 1.0
billion during the implementation period, would be financed 29 percent by internal cash
generation, 37 percent by long-term borrowing, and 34 percent by government
counterpart funds. Corporate debt is anticipated to remain less than one-third of the
capital structure during project implementation, allowing for further borrowing to finance
construction of a planned phase II wastewater treatment facility beginning in 2002.

5.21  The salient features of the future finances of (a) JWSC and (b) JMDC, based on the

assumptions used in the financial projections, and detailed operational and financial
monitoring indicators are included in Annex 14. The projected financial statements are
provided in the Report on Public Utilities Projections and Assumptions, dated March 15,
1997, which is included in the Project File (Annexj16). The organizational structure of each
utility and local sector structure are shown in Annex 11.
5.22 Assurances were obtained during negotiations that JWSC shall (a) produce for each
fiscal year commencing in fiscal year 1997, total revenues sufficient to cover its operating
and maintenance costs (before depreciation), increases in working capital, debt service,
and 20 percent of the average capital expenditures made in a three-year period (the year
preceding the reference year, the reference year itself and the year after the reference
year); and (b) incur no additional debt without the Bank's agreement, unless a reasonable
forecast shows that it would have a debt service coverage of at least 1.3 times. An
understanding was obtained during negotiations, and recorded in the minutes of
negotiations, that JWSC would limit management and meter reading staff increases during
the project implementation period in order to absorb excess administrative overhead.
5.23 Assurances were obtained during negotiations that JMDC shall (a) produce for
each fiscal year, commencing with fiscal year 1997, total revenues sufficient to cover its
operations and maintenance costs (including depreciation), and the amount by which debt
service requirements exceed the provision for depreciation; and (b) incur no additional
debt without the Bank's agreement, unless a reasonable forecast shows that it would have a
debt service coverage of at least 1.3 times.
H. DISTRICT HEATING AGENCIES
5.24 Weihai Botong Heat and Power Company Ltd. (WBHPC). When the CHP
plant operations under this project commence in 1998, WBHPC expects to receive 42
percent of its revenue from power, 43 percent from steam, and 15 percent from district
heating operations. Operating costs, in all CHP plants, however, are highly dependent on
the price of coal, since coal is expected to account for about 80 percent of operating costs.
Capital investment of Y 343 million, increases in working capital and cash changes
during the project implementation period would be financed through 24 percent internal
cash generation, 40 percent through long-term borrowing, and 35 percent from
government counterpart funds. During the project implementation period, debt levels
reach as high as 82 percent of the capital structure of the company and debt servicing is
tight, limiting the company's ability to weather downturns in projected sales volumes.
While billing for district heating and cooling services is expected to occur at the
beginning of each respective season, expectations are that users would on average remit
payment within one month. Billing and collections for steam would occur monthly based
on actual usage and annual winter heating fees are expected to be fully collected before
the heat is supplied to customers. WBHPC would be selling 100 percent of its heat (hot
water) production to Weihai South Heating Supply Company, an existing heat



- 44 -
distribution company responsible for servicing both existing and future domestic
households. In order to protect the financial viability of WBHPC, an understanding was
obtained during negotiations that Weihai Municipality would assist Weihai South Heat
Supply Company to meet its obligations to WBHPC should the company not be in a
position to pay WBHPC from its own sources within 45 days of receipt of heating bills
issues by WBHPC.
5.25  Yantai Heating Supply Company Limited (YHSC).  YHSC's operating
revenues would be received solely through sale of space heating to residential and
commercial users in Yantai's city center. Service is expected to commence in 1997, but
not reach full coverage until 2000. The company's main operating expense is purchased
heat, since only 15 percent of required heat for heat production is produced in-house. The
project's capital expenditures, increases in working capital and changes in cash would be
financed 21 percent through internal cash generation, 43 percent through long-term
borrowing, and 36 percent through government counterpart funds. Billing would take
place at the beginning of each heating season and customers are expected to pay for the
season's service within one month.
5.26 The salient features of the future finances of (a) WBHPC and (b) YHSC, based on
the indices and exchange rates mentioned in para. 3.9, are highlighted in Table 5.3. The
assumptions used in the financial projections, and detailed operational and financial
monitoring indicators are included in Annex 14. The projected financial statements are
provided in the Report on Public Utilities Financial Projections and Assumptions dated
March 15, 1997, which is included in the Project File (Annex 16). The organizational
structure of each utility and local sector structure are shown in Annex I1.
5.27 During negotiations, assurances were obtained that WBHPC and YHSC shall
(a) produce for each fiscal year, commencing with fiscal year 1997 in the case of YHSC,
and fiscal year 1998 in the case of WBHPC, total revenues sufficient to cover their
operations and maintenance costs (including depreciation), and the amount by which debt
service requirements exceed the provision for depreciation; and (b) incur no additional
debt without the Bank's agreement, unless a reasonable forecast shows that the entities
would have a debt service coverage of at least 1.3 times.
5.28 During negotiations, assurances were obtained that JWSC, JMDC, WBHPC and
YHSC wouldprepare, before September 30, 1998, and in each of the followingfiscal years,
forecasts satisfactory to the Bank and would (a) review whether they would meet the
covenanted requirements set forth above in such year and the followingfiscal year; and (b)
furnish the results of such reviews to the Bank If any such review would show that an
entity would not meet the requirements set out above, the entity would take all necessary
measures, including adjustments to the structure of its tariffs and charges, in order to meet
the requirements.

I. ENVIRONMENTAL POLLUTION PREVENTION AND CONTROL
5.29   As some of the priority pollution control subprojects are, by nature, not financially
productive, the financial appraisal would focus on the long-term financial health of the
enterprises. The key considerations in this assessment include the enterprise's development
goals and strategy, product mix and market analysis, investment requirements and related
financing arrangements, and projected financial performance in terms of profitability,
liquidity and debt servicing capabilities. With a view to promoting prudent financial
management, the agreed subproject selection criteria call for the entity to maintain a debt
service coverage ratio of not less than 1.4 times after the proposed subproject has reached
full production capacity.



-46 -
6. ECONOMIC, SOCIAL AND ENVIRONMENTAL
CONSIDERATIONS
A. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
6.1   The project is consistent with the Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) priorities of
environmental protection and alleviation of infrastructure bottlenecks, as described in
Chapter 2, and responds to China's priorities by supporting a national pilot program to
implement a river basin approach at the Xiaoqing River in Shandong Province.
Specifically, the project would support:
(a)   the provincial and municipal environmental protection bureaus (EPBs) in
their efforts to prepare and implement a comprehensive river basin
program, including the prioritization of interventions based on their cost-
effectiveness;
(b)   the establishment of financially sustainable utilities through the creation of
new corporations with a separate budget, such as the Jinan Drainage
Company, and through a tariff policy that assures the coverage of
operational costs and debt service and an increasing contribution to future
investments;
(c)   reduction of water pollution from Jinan city through the expansion of
sewerage services and full utilization of the existing treatment capacity,
thereby protecting the urban environment and the health of their people
and those living downstream of these cities;
(d)   protection of groundwater and improvement of water supply in Jinan,
through a shift toward surface water and stabilizing of water supply even
during the dry season;
(e)   reduction of air pollution in Weihai and Yantai through more efficient
central district heating systems, replacing a large number of small
inefficient boilers and stoves; with the added benefits of minimizing
economic costs through large coal savings; and
(f)   reduction of industrial pollution through a line of credit and technical
assistance that focuses on the dissemination of cost-effective cleaner
technologies.



-47 -
B. ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
6.2  In recent years, the cost of environmental degradation caused by urban pollution
has become very apparent in Shandong Province and has translated into financial losses
and the disappearance of whole sectors, such as aquaculture in the estuary of the
Xiaoqing. In response, the Province plans to embark on a comprehensive program to
reduce pollution. Such program represents a major investment for the Province and a
challenging task of coordinating a wide array of activities and institutions.
Implementation of the program therefore requires careful prioritization of interventions to
maximize their net benefit in terms of environmental improvements.   Since
environmental benefits can only partially be translated into monetary values, often the
choice of priority interventions has to be guided by cost-effectiveness considerations.
Such a least-cost approach to achieve environmental improvement was successfully
applied in the selection of several project components. Three of the components achieve
cost-effectiveness by making better use of existing but underutilized urban infrastructure.
The proposed project would provide the critical missing link to fully utilize existing
capacity, e.g., the water supply component in Jinan would allow the existing water
treatment plant to increase capacity utilization from less than 25 percent to about 100
percent. The environmental subloans to industry, in turn, focus on cost-effective
interventions of pollution prevention and waste minimization, thereby helping industry to
achieve a satisfactory environmental performance at a lower cost compared to traditional
end-of-pipe solutions. The proposed district heating components in Weihai and Yantai
are not only the environmentally most benign solutions, but they are also the least-cost
economic alternatives before accounting for environmental benefits.
6.3  In addition to investments, Shandong Province and its cities need changes in
policy and institutional structures to achieve environmental improvements. A key
problem are urban environmental services that are priced too low to discourage waste
generation or to generate the self-financing needed to build, maintain and operate services
such as district heating or wastewater treatment. Therefore, investment choices were
made in view of supporting institutional and tariff reforms, such as the creation of the
Jinan Municipal Drainage Company (JMDC), which takes over responsibility for
wastewater from a former government bureau.
City and Sector Choices
6.4   Specific investment choices within sectors and cities responded to local priorities
and long-run municipal strategies to expand urban environmental services and to meet
minimum national ambient environmental quality standards. Three cities were selected:
Jinan, the capital of Shandong Province, which ranks fourth among the 37
"environmentally most stressed cities in China" according to a National Environmental
Protection Agency (NEPA) pollution index, was selected because of its major
environmental problems and its immediate impact on the water quality of the Xiaoqing
River. The other two cities, Yantai and Weihai, are medium-size cities that had a clearly
defined strategy to address their air pollution problems through the expansion of district



-48 -
heating and offered opportunities to demonstrate new technologies that would be relevant
to other, similarly sized cities in the province.
Credit Line for Pollution Abatement
6.5   Reducing industrial pollution is a cornerstone of the XRB program. The Province
has established a subloans facility, to be initially capitalized at $10 million, for which it
requested a Bank loan of $5 million. In line with China's new market-oriented approach
to lending for industries, the pollution prevention and control line of credit would
introduce strict financial and technical evaluation criteria, and would not offer
concessional interest rates. The line of credit would be a transitional and catalytic
financial mechanism to help overcome the credit-market constraints for environmental
investments (more fully described in the China Department's 1995 Capital Market Sector
Study). The major benefit of the facility would be the demonstration of a cost-effective
approach to pollution reduction by applying cleaner production, waste minimization, and
waste recovery techniques. The fund focuses on top-ranked small- and medium-size
major polluters that discharge into the Xiaoqing River; the pollution reduction is
estimated to be significant at an enterprise level, but, given the magnitude of industrial
pollution in the river basin, the expected impact on the water quality of the Xiaoqing
River would onjy be marginal.
Water Supply in Jinan
6.6   The purpose of the Jinan Water Supply Component is to halt groundwater
depletion while meeting the increasing water demand from Jinan's urban population and
industry. The project would achieve this by increasing the supply of surface water from
the Yellow River and by compensating for seasonal fluctuations of the river flow. The
immediate benefit to the serviced population of about 1 million is an increased water
supply of 400,000 m3/day, which would effectively reduce current water shortages. The
economic net benefit of improved water supply is about Y 150 million and the economic
rate of return (ERR) 18 percent. The long-term benefit includes a slightly reduced
groundwater extraction, which is a first step toward a return to sustainable levels of
groundwater management.
6.7   The project represents the least-cost option because of its effective use of existing
but underutilized infrastructure, its closeness to the existing water treatment plant and to
its consumers, and the contribution to a reduced groundwater extraction. Alternative
schemes were considered but were more costly or unsustainable: (a) the investment cost
of a surface water extraction scheme at Tianshan on the Yellow River, upstream of Jinan
to supply the same volume of water would be 58 percent higher and the unit cost per ton
of treated water per day would be 11 percent higher than for the proposed project. (b) If
the additional volume of water were to be supplied from groundwater, overall extraction
in the year 2000 would reach about 1.2 million m3/day, more than double the long-term
sustainable extraction volume of 500,000 m3/day. Overextraction would accelerate the
drop in the average groundwater level, which has already dropped from about 29 meters



- 49 -
(m) to less than 26 m above sea level over the last 30 years and further reduce the flow of
Jinan's famous springs. Reduced spring flow also reduces the flow of surface water
within Jinan and to the Xiaoqing River and, as a consequence, increases the concentration
of pollution from industrial and domestic effluents, specifically during the dry season. In
the long run, overextraction could lead to a irreversible loss of the groundwater resource.
6.8   Improvement in water use efficiency is a critical element in achieving the project
objectives. Industrial water demand is projected to increase from about 650,000 m3/day
in 1996 to 950,000 m3/day in 2010, assuming an ambitious annual 6 percent decline of
the water consumption per unit of industrial output. Jinan has a track record of already
having achieved, between 1990 and 1995, a reduction from 15,000 m3/Y million to 6,000
(a 20 percent per year reduction). Nonetheless, achieving such sustained water use
efficiency would require special attention to and incentives for water conservation
measures in industry. For private households, rising water tariffs are likely to encourage
water conservation: a price elasticity of -0.17 for domestic per capita water consumption
was estimated based on the last 15 years of JWSC's sales. However, rising incomes and
living standards would more than compensate this impact with an estimated per capita
income elasticity of 0.22. As a result, aggregate demand from households (including
public buildings) for which the population growth rate is estimated at 2.3 percent (1.9
percent after 2000) would increase from about 300,000 m3/day in 1995 to over 650,000
m3/day in 2010.
Jinan Wastewater
6.9   The Jinan Wastewater Component is a first step to reduce the city's contribution
to the pollution of the Xiaoqing River. The proposed project would provide the missing
connection to the sewerage network and enhance its collection capacity to allow the STW
to operate at its full capacity of 220,000 m3/day, or about 45 percent of the total waste
stream. The project has the immediate benefit to provide improved sewerage services to
a service population of about 1 million, and to reduce Jinan's pollution load into the
Xiaoqing River by about 32,000 tons of chemical oxygen demand (COD) annually.
While this would reduce COD concentration in the river by almost 100 mg/l (or 45
percent), river quality would still exceed Class V Standards of 25 mg/l by a factor of 5
and make it necessary for Jinan to further increase its treatment capacity after completion
of this first phase. Within the city, the project complements the long-term effort of the
water supply project to ensure a sustainable groundwater supply because reduced quantity
and concentration of surface water pollution would also reduce groundwater pollution.
6.10  The quantification of the economic benefits, which are largely environmental and
health improvements, poses challenging analytical problems.  But even without
quantification, it is evident that the utilization of the idle sewage treatment plant makes
this investment highly cost-effective. The annual reduction of 32,000 tons COD is
achieved through an incremental investment of Y 264 million over a lifetime of at least
15 years. By contrast, without the project, Jinan already incurs an annual cost of



-50 -
approximately Y 15 million for depreciation and maintenance of the idle STP without
achieving any environmental or other benefit.
District Heating
6.11  The proposed district heating components in Yantai and Weihai are aimed at
reducing air pollution by replacing inefficient small boilers and stoves with high-
efficiency district heating networks to serve residential and industrial heating and steam
processing needs. The projects would reduce SO2 and TSP emissions through a
combination of more efficient heat generation, better pollution abatement equipment and
higher stacks than the existing heating facilities have. The immediate benefits are heat
services to a population of 400,000 in Yantai. In Weihai, they would provide heat
services to a population of 150,000 in addition to supplying steam for industries and
power cogeneration.
6.12  Three approaches were chosen for the economic analysis: (a) least-cost analysis
of the district heating projects and two other options in terms of their heat generation and
distribution costs; (b) environmental impact comparison, including coal consumption and
emissions of SO2, TSP, and carbon dioxide (CO2); and (c) intangible economic cost and
benefit considerations. The following three Options were considered when determining
the least-cost option for meeting the estimated future demand of residential heat,
industrial steam, and cooling in the project areas by the year 2000: (a) Option 1-
business as usual, i.e., the provision of heat through a combination of small boilers and
stoves, as today; (b) Options 2-small boilers only, i.e., the replacement of all stoves with
small boilers and continued use of the existing small boilers; and (c) Option 3-district
heating, the complete replacement of existing stoves and small boilers; this is the
proposed project.
Least-Cost Analysis
6.13  Results for Weihai. Introduction of district heating in Weihai's Economic
Development District would lead to the replacement of over 18,000 stoves and about 100
small boilers by the year 2000. Savings under the district heating option amount to
Y 0.35 billion if compared to the business-as-usual option and over Y 0.5 billion if
compared to the small boiler option. The respective ERR on these net savings are 28 and
41 percent, not including the savings from land reclamation. The economic advantage of
district heating appears robust even if compared against a "low-heat" alternative, whereby
stove owners would continue heating their rooms on average at a low room temperatures
of 10 to 12C during four hours a day only, thereby reducing their coal consumption to
one-third of te coal rtquired for constant 18C room temperature. District heating also
prevails as the preferred option if compared to much improved small boilers with an
increased efficiency of 65 percent, up from 50 percent. Table 6.1 summarizes these
findings.

6.14   Results for Yantai. With the introduction of district heating in Yantai, 64,000
household stoves and 200 small boilers would have been replaced by the year 2000.
District heating would generate economic savings of Y 0.2 billion or Y 0.5 billion,
respectively, if compared to the other two options, which are largely the result of fuel
efficiency: district heating consumes 50 percent less with respect to Option 1, and 30
percent less than Option 2. Investment costs are lower, too. The ERR on these savings
are 24 percent or 66 percent, respectively. Further large savings are likely to result from
the reclamation of valuable urban land that is currently occupied by small boilers and that
would become available as these boilers are replaced by district heating. These land
reclamation benefits were not included in the analysis because their actual market value is
uncertain. Table 6.2 provides a summary of the results. Economic benefits are robust,
even when the construction of a new CHP plant (greenfield project) is considered.

Environmental Impact
6.15   Second, the three options were compared with regard to their environmental
impact. The district heating achieves reductions in S02, TSP, and C02 emissions
compared to the other options. In Yantai, annual emissions of S02 and TSP under the
district heating option are only 3 and 4 percent of what would be emitted under the
"business-as-usual" scenario, i.e., stoves and boilers. Figure 6.1 compares the economic
and environmental performance of the three options for Yantai using the business-as-

6.17  In order to assess the full cost of each option, the economic cost of air pollution
should be included. However, the methodologies to calculate the economic cost of air
pollution are still being debated and reliable data on health impact and exposure are not
available in Shandong. Some information on health costs related to air pollution is
available internationally, but this information needs to be adjusted to local circumstances.
In order to illustrate the impact of benefits from reduced air pollution damage on the
overall economic benefits, a simplified approach (so-called benefit-transfer approach) has
been applied, using data from Europe (the United Kingdom) adjusted by the ratio of GNP
in China and the United Kingdom. Based on this approach, the following benefits per ton
removed were applied: Y 440 for SO2, Y 3,800 for TSP, and Y 1.6 for CO2. As a result,
project costs increased by 15 percent to 50 percent for the various options. Considering
pollution cost makes district heating economically even more attractive: The district
heating option generates additional savings of Y 130 million in the form of reduced
emissions (or Y 90 million if compared to option 2). TSP reductions represent the largest
share (over 80 percent) of these savings, followed by SO2 (about 15 percent) and CO2
(less than 5 percent). Including the cost of pollution does not change the ranking of
options in this case, but points to additional benefits of the project for the local
environment (SO2, TSP) and the global environment (CO2 reduction).
6.18   Intangible Cost and Benefit Considerations.  These include the following:
(a) height of stacks of the two district heating plants, which are over 1 00 m high, allowing
for a better dispersion of emissions than the stacks of existing small boilers, which is
typically 20 to 35 m high; (b) tariff adjustments, which would lead to a sustainable
heating operation that fully recovers its costs; (c) demand-side management: the project
would promote the introduction of individual metering for each household unit. A
demonstration project would be implemented in Yantai that would test individual
metering and billing procedures.



- 54 -
6.19  In summary, the proposed District Heating solutions present a win-win
opportunity: the package of fuel efficiency, least-cost economic cost, and environmental
benefits makes the proposed project components attractive from an economic
environmental management, public finance, and an utility perspective.

Overall Program and Capacity Building
6.20  This project represents a major investment for Shandong Province.  These
economic costs are justified by environmental and social benefits that would be generated
through the project, including improved water quality in the Xiaoqing River and air
quality in two cities, protection of groundwater resources, and improved services for
water, wastewater, and heating.
6.21  Efforts have been made to quantify these benefits, but only an approximation and
rough estimates are possible based on available infonnation and current methodologies.
The results indicate that the project components contribute to environmnental
improvement through localized reduction of pollution levels and protection of natural
resources. When implemented, it would help to reduce downstream health damages and
production losses, such as the disappearance of aquaculture in the estuary of the Xiaoqing
River, which represents an estimated annual loss of more than Y 200 million. While the
quantum of economic benefits can only be estimated, it is clear that the project would
generate a positive net enviromnmental and social benefit.
6.22  The economic attractiveness of the project, beyond its environmental benefits, is
helped by the fact that several components also generate net economic savings in form of
reduced resource use, such as coal consumption for heating. These savings are generated



- 55 -
through technological improvements, such as increased fuel efficiency, and economies of
scale. Utility operators receive part of these economic savings in form of reduced costs
of operation. District-heating projects therefore represent true win-win opportunities that
make them attractive both from a society and a profit-making perspective. This
attractiveness should greatly help in the dissemination of this project type to other cities.
6.23   However, taken by themselves, the project components would only have a
localized impact and a marginal effect on overall environmental conditions in the
province. Only if the larger program, of which this project forms part, is implemented
and successful innovations introduced under the project are replicated in other places,
would a noticeable environmental improvement be achieved; this serves to underline the
importance of the Institutional Strengthening and Training component in strengthening
local capacity for implementing the larger program and applying available resources
effectively to maximize their environmental benefit. The value added by this component
would be the transfer of relevant cross-country experience and their adaptation to the
specific needs of the province. For example, the long-term study would provide support
for efficient pollution load allocation and its regulatory enforcement along the Xiaoqing
River. Choice of appropriate water quality levels has a major impact on the cost of the
program, and hence its viability: adopting overambitious targets would drain resources
away from higher impact interventions. Developing the necessary information base to
better evaluate the full economic cost and benefits of such interventions would be a
tangible benefit of the Institutional Strengthening component.
6.24 Investments in pollution reduction would not be sustainable without more
efficient use of resources. For example, water consumption in paper industry reaches 400
to 600 m3 per ton of product, way above the industry standard that is achievable with
current technologies (about 50 to 200 m3/ton). Excessive water consumption increases
the waste stream and requires higher investment in larger treatment plants. Low water
prices paid by some industries support such behavior. By effectively removing subsidies
for public utility services through the introduction of cost recovering tariff policies, the
project not only supports the financial viability of the utility but also creates a stronger
incentive for the efficient use of natural resources. Furthermore, by internalizing the cost
of pollution abatement of each project component into the tariffs, users have a stronger
incentive for the efficient use of these urban services.
C. AFFORDABILITY
6.25 As part of the Government's policy to establish financially viable utilities, tariffs
for urban environmental services would be raised to a level where operational costs are
covered and a contribution to future investments can be made. Since these utilities
provide essential services, the impact of these tariff increases on the poor was assessed,
using for reference the 1995 income of persons in "very poor households" as defined in
Shandong Province's 1995 Statistical Yearbook.  For water and wastewater, the
combined annual cost does not exceed 3 percent at the suggested 1997 and 2000 tariff
level. For district heating, on the other hand, the share is 8.5 percent and drops to 6.5



- 56 -
percent in 2000. While this is a considerable burden to poor households, the cost is
comparable to current expenditures for heating a coal stove. Even at drastically lower
heating standards, annual coal consumption is about 1.0 ton at an average cost of Y 488
per stove. If divided among two income earners, the annual cost is Y 240 per person,
compared to Y 230 per person for district heating.
D. PROJECT RISKS
6.26  The Bank-supported components of Shandong Environment Project form part of a
broader strategy to secure environmental improvements in the XRB and enhanced well-
being in Jinan and other project cities. Each component, if effectively implemented,
would make a local contribution to the regional objective of the River Basin. However,
to have a basinwide impact, the institutional innovations, the new approaches to pollution
management, and demonstrated technologies of each of the components need to be
replicated and complemented by sustained efforts across sectors and throughout the
Basin.
6.27  Risks surrounding the project have therefore been divided into two broad
categories: Short/Medium-Term Project Risks and Long-Term Strategic Risks. The
former center around the immediate requirements of the project and the successful
implementation of its components. The latter center around the overall impact of the
project and relate to the sustainability of environmental improvements in the XRB.
Short/Medium-Term Project Risks
6.28  The principal risks directly associated with the implementation of the project and
the achievement of immediate project outputs can be grouped in environmental/
technical, financial and institutional issues (as summarized in Table 6.3 below).
Long-Term Strategic Risks
6.29 Increase Environmental Pressures. Rapid economic and population growth,
together with urbanization and industrialization, would continue to exercise
environmental stress in the XRB. The carrying capacity of this ecological system is not
yet fully understood nor is the impact of the current developments. This limits the ability
of environmental management to give clear directions where, what type, and how much
development is compatible with the natural environment and hence sustainable.
6.30  Areas of potential conflict include the push for industrial growth and need to
protect the environment. Given the high returns on investments in a fast-growing
economy, pollution reduction is competing for scarce capital with high opportunity costs.
Only a systematic internalization of environmental externalities, as has started at a low
level with the introduction of pollution charges, would resolve this apparent conflict by
reflecting environment-related expenditures in the cost of doing business.



- 57 -
6.31   The investment components would, within their scope, mitigate some of these
risks by improving resources pricing and the provision of environmental services. The
technical assistance under the project would then play a key role in disseminating these
experiences and assist the Province to integrate them in the broader context of the river
basin. For example, the Long-Term Study for Xiaoqing River would provide planning
tools and data to anticipate and manage environmental risks (see Table 6.4 for a summary
of long-term risks).
