7. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
7.1   The following assurances were obtained at negotiations:
(a)   JWSC would, not later than December 31, 2000, strengthen its distribution
systems to handle an increased supply of about 400,000 m3/day of fully
treated water to Jinan (para. 2.12);
(b)   SP would implement the Institutional Strengthening and Training
component in accordance with a schedule acceptable to the Bank and
discuss and agree any revisions to the schedule with the Bank (para. 3.4);
(c)   The municipalities would onlend part of the loan proceeds to their
respective water, wastewater and district heating utility companies, on
terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank (para. 3.12);
(d)   SP would onlend to participating enterprises, on terms and conditions
satisfactory to the Bank [para. 3.14];
(e)   SP would allocate to XRBSL about Y 42 million over the three fiscal
years commencing 1997, depositing annually to the account of XRBSL by
June 30, of the following years-at least Y 16.5 million in 1997, Y 12.5
million in 1998, and Y 12.5 million in 1999 [para. 3.14];
(f)   SP would carry out or cause to be carried out (i) the resettlement of
persons affected by the project in a manner and according to the
Resettlement Action Plans satisfactory to the Bank; and (ii) report
quarterly on such progress (para. 3.36);
(g)   SP would cause the project agencies to carry out, in a manner satisfactory
to the Bank, the findings of the Environmental Assessment and
implementation plan (para. 3.38);
(h)   Each implementing agency would prepare quarterly project progress
reports; SPPMO would then send a consolidated report to the Bank by the
thirtieth of the following month, commencing February 1998 (para. 3.39);
(i)   A mid-term  review  of project progress would be undertaken by
September 30, 2000, and the agreed recommendations implemented
thereafter (para. 3.41);
(j)   SP would maintain SPPMO and the municipal project offices throughout
implementation, with functions and responsibilities satisfactory to the



- 60 -
Bank, and with competent staff in adequate numbers for the duration of
the project (para. 4.3);
(k)   SP would carry out, or cause to be carried out (i) a time-bound Xiaoqing
River Basin Environmental Action Plan; (ii) an environmental data
collection update with respect to water quality and management in XRB,
by June 30, 1998; and, upon completion, review the data collected with the
Bank; and (iii) time-bound Environmental Action Plans for the three top-
ranked polluters in the XRB (para. 4.15);
(1)   SP would arrange for the following annual audits to be submitted to the
Bank within six months after the end of the financial year: (i) audit of the
project accounts maintained by SPPMO; (ii) audit of the Special Account;
(iii) audit of statements of expenditure (SOEs); and (iv) audits of the
financial statements of JWSC; JMDC, WBHPC, YHSC and XRBSL (para.
5.7);
(m)   Commencing with fiscal year 1997, JWSC shall (i) annually produce total
revenues sufficient to cover operations and maintenance costs (before
depreciation), increases in working capital, debt service, and 20 percent of
the average capital expenditures made in a three-year period (the year
preceding the reference year, the reference year itself, and the year after
the reference year); and (ii) incur no additional debt without the Bank's
agreement, unless a reasonable forecast shows that it would have a debt
service coverage of at least 1.3 times (para. 5.22);
(n)   Commencing with fiscal year 1997, JMDC shall (i) annually produce total
revenues sufficient to cover its operations and maintenance costs
(including depreciation), and the amount by which debt service
requirements exceed the provision for depreciation; and (ii) incur no
additional debt without the Bank's agreement, unless a reasonable forecast
shows that the entity would have a debt service coverage of at least 1.3
times (para. 5.23);
(o)   Commencing with fiscal year 1997 in the case of YHSC, and with fiscal
year 1998 in the case of WBHPC shall (i) annually produce total revenues
sufficient to cover operations and maintenance costs (including
depreciation), and the amount by which debt service requirements exceed
the provision for depreciation; and (ii) incur no additional debt without the
Bank's agreement, unless a reasonable forecast shows that the entity
would have a debt service coverage of at least 1.3 times (para. 5.27); and
(p)   The water supply, wastewater and district heating utility companies would
prepare, before September 30, 1998, and in each of the following fiscal
years, forecasts satisfactory to the Bank, and would (i) review whether
they would meet the covenanted requirements set forth above in such year

People's Republic of China.

- 63 -                    ANNEX 1
ANNEX 1: BACKGROUND TO SHANDONG PROVINCE AND
PROJECT CITIES
1.    The economy of Shandong province, which has a 1995 population of some 87
million (32 percent urban) has grown rapidly over the past decade. The 1995 per capita
GDP was Y 5,760, with an average annual growth rate of about 15.7 percent during the
period of 1990-95. The proportion of financial income to the total GDP had decreased
from 10.4 percent in 1985 to 7.7 percent in 1995. The average income of urban residents
and farmers in 1995 was Y 3,959 and Y 1,715, respectively.
2.    The principal industrial sectors in financial terms in Shandong in the past decade
include energy, chemical, textile, machine, and the food industry. The average annual
growth rates of industrial output for TVIEs have been extremely high over the period
1990-95 (35.4 percent for township enterprises and 36.1 percent for village enterprises),
much higher than that for state-owned enterprises (10.3 percent). TVIEs accounted for 63
percent of industrial output in 1995.
3.    The Province has been one of the engines driving China's impressive economic
growth over the past decade. This was primarily the result of substantial increases in
agricultural productivity and industrial output. The latter took place to a large extent in
Shandong's urban areas, particularly in Jinan, the capital city, and in the coastal cities of
Qingdao, Weihai and Yantai. As a consequence, Jinan City, for example, suffers from
coal emission pollution and acid rain from its abundance of industrial and residential
boilers, which is exacerbated by the city's adverse topographic location, with over 200
inversions per year. Thus, in terms of air quality, Jinan ranks fourth among the 37 most
environmentally stressed cities in China, constricting economic development, according
to NEPA. Most other cities in the province are also failing to meet national standards on
air pollution. The situation is especially alarming during the heating season (four months
of the year).
4.    Shandong Province has a rain-sparse climate with a large amount of the water
supply derived from underground sources. Some of these aquifers are being increasingly
polluted and reduced below replenishment amounts and domestic and industrial water has
to be extracted from often polluted rivers, rainfed reservoirs or piped from distant water
sources. Some cities, e.g., Jinan, Qingdao and Zibo, have to rely on costly water
transmission from the Yellow River. At the same time, most rivers and lakes, especially
the Xiaoqing River and Nanshi Lake, are heavily polluted, aggravating water shortages
and restricting long-term economic development.



-64 -                      ANNEX 1
Jinan
5.    Jinan Municipality, located in western Shandong, has an urban area of 2,119 km2
and a population of 1.5 million. The core area of the city is 1 10 km2, with a population of
1.5 million.
6.    The GDP of Jinan Municipality amounted to Y 48.1 billion in 1995, with an
annual growth rate of 15.2 percent. The per capita GDP was Y 8,884 in 1995, ranked
sixth among the 17 municipalities and prefectures in Shandong. Jinan's industrial base
includes machinery manufacture, steel, nonferrous metals, chemicals, building materials,
coal, textile, light industry, and food and beverage. In 1995, Jinan had 2,276 industrial
enterprises (county level and above). The total industrial and agricultural output in 1995
was Y 45.7 and Y 11.4 billion, respectively.
Weihai
7.    Weihai Municipality, located at the eastern end of the Shandong Peninsula, with
an area of 731 km2 and a population of 438,000, had a GDP of Y 33.7 billion in 1995,
with an annual growth rate of 14 percent. Weihai's industrial base includes light
industry, electronic, machinery manufacture, textile, and chemicals. The total industrial
and agricultural output in 1995 was Y 41.0 and 15.7 billion, respectively.
Yantai
8.    Yantai Municipality, located in the northeastern part of the Shandong Peninsula
with an area of 13,745 km2 and an urban population of 1.5 million in 1995, had a GDP of
Y 57.5 billion in 1995, with an annual growth rate of 15.5 percent, ranked in second place
in the province (after Qingdao). The main industrial sectors includes light industry, food
and beverage, textile, machinery manufacture, construction materials, coal, and
electronics. In 1994, Yantai Municipality had 2,558 industrial enterprises (township level
and above) and 8,191 village-level industrial enterprises.  The total industrial an
agricultural output in 1995 was Y 51.3 and Y 24.6 billion, respectively.



- 65 -                    ANNEX2
ANNEX 2: DETAILED PROJECT DESCRIPTION
1.    The components of the proposed project investments are described in detail below,
followed by a summary in tabular form.
JINAN
2.    Jinan City, capital of Shandong Province, had a total industrial productive output
of Y 41 billion in 1995, which is projected to be around Y 74.5 billion in the year 2000.
Major industries include machinery, electrical, textile, petrochemical, paper and foods
and beverage production.
3.    The Jinan City Master Plan covering the period 1994-2010 was prepared by the
Municipal Planning Institute and Planning Bureau and issued in January 1996. The
designated city center area extends to about 600 km2, bounded by the Juye and Yufu
Rivers to the east and west, respectively, the Yellow River to the north, and Jiwang Road
at the south. The designated "central" area includes the city center, together with the
peripheral zones of Dongjia, Wangsheren, Dajin and Dangjia. The city center population
was reported at 1.47 million in 1995, and is projected to reach 1.95 and 2.35 million in
2000 and 2010, respectively. These projections include a floating population estimated at
0.25 and 0.30 million in those years.
4.    Water Supply System.  The Jinan Water Supply Company (JWSC) was
established in 1936 to provide a supply of about 7,600 m3/d at that time, through 40 km
of pipe network for about 1,700 residential and industrial units in the commercial area of
the city. The system has been progressively extended, particularly in the past 15 years or
so, with the present installed design capacity reported around 1,080,000 m3/d, although
the actual available supply is restricted to some 740,000 m3/d due to resource constraints.
The existing water distribution network now covers an area of some 364 km2 and extends
to about 1,150 km with pipes in the range 100 to 1,500 mm diameter, reported to include
cast iron, ductile iron, reinforced and prestressed concrete pipe materials. The ground
elevation across the supply area varies in the order of 90 m, and 11 booster pump stations
of capacity of 321,000 m3/d are located in the city.
5.    Groundwater has traditionally been the main water resource for the city.
However, this resource has been progressively supplemented in recent years by supplies
from impounding reservoirs and direct abstractions from the Yellow River in order to
control the "mining" of groundwater caused by overabstraction of this resource. The
water resource situation is summarized as follows:

6.     Water Demand. The water demand projections through the planning horizon of
year 2010 have been estimated based on the following broad assumptions: population
served; industrial output value; and other uses (including public facilities, firefighting and
system losses). The population figures (including floating population) of 1.95 and 2.35
million in years 2000 and 2010 respectively have been adopted. Per capita demands of
190 lI/c/d (actual in 1995), rising to 240 and 300 I/c/d in years 2000 and 2010 have been
assumed; these trends are in line with other similar urban centers in China. The
respective figures for floating population are 100 and 130 l/c/d in years 2000 and 2010.
Industrial output in the supply area is projected to reach about Y 132.3 billion in year
2010, although the unit industrial water consumption is expected to fall progressively
over the planning period from 60 m3/Y 10,000 of output value in 1995 to 25 m by 2010
(about 5 percent per year), in line with national industrial trends. Other water uses are
expected to reduce from 18 percent (actual in 1995) to 16 percent in 2000 and 15 percent
by 2010, reflecting improved system operational efficiency and reduced system losses.

length.



- 69-                        ANNEX2
JINAN WASTEWATER SYSTEM
11.   A Sewerage Master plan was originally prepared in 1983; this was updated in
1993 and provides the basis for the sewerage planning for the city. The urban area of
Jinan is divided into seven catchments comprising Jiqi, Jiluo, Darning Lake, Liuhangtou,
Huangtai Qili River, Wangsheren and Danjia catchments totaling about 140 km2 in area.
12.   Existing Wastewater System.  The existing sewer system  comprises about
145 km of pipes in the catchments of Jiluo, Darning, Liuhangtou and Huangtai, in sizes
between 300 and 1,500 mm diameter, mostly of concrete pipe construction, with small
lengths of brick culverts and pipes. There are a number of pump stations, the most
significant of which is the Juluo Bridge station, built in 1985, of about 220,000 m3/d
design capacity, to lift sewage to the Jilan Wastewater Treatment Plant (SPT). This
station is presently underutilized pending the construction of a new intercepting sewer to
convey the flow to the SPT.
13.   The present wastewater flow from all seven catchments in the city is about
680,000 m3/day, comprising about 30 percent domestic and 70 percent industrial flows.
The existing sewerage system is partly combined and partly separate, and is seriously
inadequate with large parts of the 145 km of main and secondary sewers not functioning
satisfactorily, due to siltation, and blocking of the inverted siphons at river crossings. All
wastewater flows discharge directly or indirectly into watercourses within the city or to
the Xiaoqing River through existing sewers. As a result, the water quality in water
courses and lakes within the city and the Xiaoqing River has deteriorated to below
National Surface Water Class 5 Standard. The Wastewater Development Plan for the city
is summarized below.

Liuhangtou and Huangtai. The objectives are to: fully utilize the existing capacity of the
Jinan STW; improve the watercourses and natural environment in the city; protect
groundwater resources from pollution; reduce pollution of the Daming Lake; and improve
the water quality of Xiaoqing River. As indicated above, the second phase of this STW
would be built during 2001-15, meanwhile, excess flows would continue to spill over into
the Xiaoqing River.
16.   Proposed Wastewater Component ($36.5 million). The proposed component
would comprise the following works:
(a)    Construction of about 18 km of main interceptor, trunk sewers and force
mains in sizes about 600 to 2,000 mm diameter, inverted siphons and
ancillary facilities, including existing wastewater treatment plant
rehabilitation and commissioning.
(b)   Construction of about 17 km of secondary and link sewers of about 500 to
1,600 mm diameter, inverted siphons and ancillary facilities.
(c)    Construction of pump station of capacity about 100,000 m3/d for
wastewater and about 81,000 m3/day for storm  drainage, including
screens, pumping equipment and ancillary facilities.
(d)   Property connections throughout sewered area, in sizes to 300 mm
diameter, totaling about 3 km in length.



-71-                      ANNEX2
(e)   Sewer Cleaning and Safety Equipment for use throughout the city,
including bucket cleaning equipment, hoists, transportation and safety
equipment.
(f)   Utility vehicles for operational use.
WEIHAI
17.   Weihai City, with a population of 438,000, has two thermal power plants that
supply heat and electricity. A new plant is required to meet demand in the south of the
city center in a developing industrial zone. Some of the steam load of the present
industry (40 tons/ hour) served by No. 1 and No. 2 plants can also be supplied from south
to vacate heat capacity (30 MW) for house heating in the city center.
18.   The plant would involve 3x75 tons/hour fluidized-bed boilers and two 12 MW
double-extraction turbogenerator sets.
19.   About 40 percent of capacity is reserved for residential heating corresponding to
about 20 percent of annual heat sales. The rest is supplied to industry. Since the southern
area of the city, where the plant is located, would, in the future, be the center of the city's
residential growth, the ratio is likely to change in the future in favor of residential load.
20.   Proposed Weihai District Heating Component ($42.9 million). The proposed
component would comprise the following works:
(a)   Combined Heat and Power Plant of about 24 MW  electricity and
120 MW heat capacity;
(b)   Heat and Steam Networks comprising about 13.7 km of steam network
of about 75 tons/hour * 1.0 Mpa capacity and hot water network of about
14.2 km of about 68 MW capacity;
(c)   Heat Exchanger Stations comprising about 10 stations of varying
capacity averaging about 6 MW.
YANTAI
21.   Yantai City has a population of 1.4 million people. The project would comprise
the construction of hot water distribution network in Zhifu District that is in the center of
the city. The network length would be 30 kIn, and it would serve 8.1 million M2 of
housing and public buildings connected to 41 heat substations. The capacity of the
system would be some 500 MW and annual heat sales some 770,000 Gal. The primary
network load density is thus some 17 MW/km.
22.   The main heat supply to the network would be from an existing power plant
owned and operated by Huang Bo-Hai Thermal Power Co. Ltd., a joint venture of three



-72-                      ANNEX2
parties: (a) Shandong Electric Power Bureau; (b) Yantai Municipality, and (c) a Hong
Kong-based investor group owned by the Bank of China.
23.   The power plant has three small old condensing turbines and two newly installed
100 MW (electrical) extraction-condensing turbines that would supply some 300 MW of
heat through a newly built main heat exchanger station.
24.   The project would also involve the installation of peak boilers near to the power
plant for peak load production.
25.   Proposed Yantai District Heating Component ($46.1 million). The proposed
component would comprise the following works:
(a)   Heating Network comprising about 30 km of pipework in sizes between
200 and 900 mm diameter, with a capacity of 500 MW;
(b)   Heat Exchanger Stations comprising about 42 stations of varying
capacities, averaging about 10 MW, and the main heat exchanger and
pumping station at the power plant;
(c)   Boiler Plant comprising boilers and associated equipment and facilities,
of about 112 MW capacity.
Xiaoqing River Basin Environmental Pollution Control Subloans Facility (XRBSL)
26.   Proposed Component ($10.0 million).  Environmental Pollution Control
Subloans would be made for environmental improvement measures of small- and
medium-size enterprises, located in the Xiaoqing River Basin. Projects under the subloan
fund would be restricted to a maximum of $1.0 million.
27.   Subloan processing and approvals would be covered by operational procedures
which are drafted. SEPB and SFB have established the Management Office, and the
China Investment Bank, Jinan branch, has been selected as the financial agent handling
appraisal of project proposals, procurement and disbursement of the subloans.
Institutional Strengthening, Construction Supervision and Training
28.   Proposed Component ($3.9 million). The component would comprise a number
of main composite packages for the various municipalities and agencies, covering
institutional development, construction supervision and training, as described below,
together with an allowance for support for provincial and sector city agencies, master
planning, and other programs of assistance to be further defined. Details are set out
below and in the Institutional Development, Financial Management Improvement,
Construction Supervision and Training Table, showing estimated national and
international person-months and other supporting relevant information. The scope of



-73 -                     ANNEX2
work and draft terms of reference for the main assignments were reviewed by the Bank at
negotiations, and placed in the Project File (see Annex16).
PACKAGES A AND B: INSTITUTIONAL DEVELOPMENT, FINANCIAL MANAGEMENT
IMPROVEMENT, CONSTRUCTION SUPERVISION AND TRANING FOR EXECUTING
AGENCIES OF JINAN, WEIHAI AND YANTAI MUNICIPALITIES
Objectives
29.   The broad objectives of the Packages A and B are to:
(a)   strengthen the executing (district heating, water and wastewater)
organizations in institutional and financial management, and the related
bureaus and divisions;
(b)   strengthen the overall provincial financial management and policy-making
in Shandong; and
(c)   ensure the efficient implementation of the project while strengthening
sector institutions.
Scope of Work
30.   The services to be provided under these assignments are for substantial
multidiscipline support programs for the respective agencies and would include:
(a)   strengthening of organizations through development of appropriate
management procedures;
(b)   provision of improved accounting systems, management information
systems, and training in district heating, water and wastewater
management operations, and computer training for accounting staff;
(c)   district heating, water and wastewater management utility pricing;
(d)   provincial financial management;
(e)   quality assurance training for materials production;
(f)   construction supervision;
(g)   wastewater treatment plant operations training;
(h)   skills upgrading for planning and analysis of development options; and
(i)   assistance to improve product technology.



-74-                      ANNEX2
PACKAGE C: ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION BUREAU
Objective
31.   The Institutional Strengthening and Training Report prepared by SEPB provides a
comprehensive assessment of the needs for SEPB and Jinan and Weihai Municipal EPBs,
which are summarized as follows: (a) training in environmental monitoring, information
systems, cleaner production, and fund management both in China and abroad; and
(b) laboratory and monitoring equipment, and office equipment.  In addition to the
above, the project would support the strengthening of implementation capacity to
undertake EIA and RAP assessments.
PACKAGE D: TRAINING AND MANAGEMENT INFORMATION SYSTEMS (MIS) SUPPORT
Objective
32.   The objective of Package D is to support the institutional development of project
entities and executing agencies through structured programs of "in-country" and
"overseas" training and organized programs of "on-the-job" training. The package would
also include the installation of a management information system, to facilitate project
coordination and the monitoring of project implementation by the SPPMO; as well as
equipment in support of the Training and MIS objectives.
Scope of Work
33.   The package would be managed by the SPPMO on behalf of the project's
executing agencies. The following products and services would be procured: (a)
training; (b) technical assistance in support of training; (c) installation and training for the
operation of a Management Information System; and (d) equipment in support of the
package.
34.   Training would be conducted in China and procured abroad in order to improve
the functional areas of agency operations listed in the Summary Training Plan (in Project
File). The sequence and content of the training program would be adjusted annually,
based on a rolling two-year plan . The training plan for 1997 and the indicative plan for
1998 have been completed and the Terms of Reference for the Overseas Study and
Investigation/Study tours would be prepared accordingly.
Dl: Training
35.   This part of the package would include the following elements:
(a)   Training in China of about 1,113 personnel (1,308.4 person-months) from
project entities and executing agencies at Shandong training institutions
and at training institutions located in other provinces of China;
(b)   Training abroad for about 38 persons (66.5 person-months) in specially
organized or regularly scheduled courses of training, according to specific



-75 -                               ANNEX2
needs identified in the course of preparation and defined in the IST Report
(part of Project File). and to terms of reference on file at SPPMO;
(c)     Investigation/Study Tours for about 85 persons (52.3 person-months) in
organized technical visits and awareness sessions according to specific
needs identified in the course of preparation and defined in the IST Report
(part of Project File) and to terms of reference on file at SPPMO;

D3: MIS Support
38.   In order to manage and control the various components of the Shandong
Environment project (finance, loan disbursements, tracking of construction supervision,
IST, etc.) SPPMO would establish a project-wide Management Information System
(MIS). The MIS would be based on the Project Implementation Plan , to be finalized by
SPPMO, and would include a set of performance indicators for each component of the
project. The services to be provided under this element would be contracted as part of a
single package , in conjunction with the TA for Training services and provision of
Training abroad and Investigation/ Study Tours.
D4: Equipment
39.   Equipment for a total value of about $0.23 million (Y 1.9 million) would be
obtained on behalf of the executing agencies as part of this package. This equipment
would support training, agency strengthening and MIS support.  The schedule of
-equipment to be procured under the package is included as part of the IST Report.

-95 -                      ANNEX6
ANNEX 6: LAND ACQUISITION AND RESETTLEMENT
ACTION PLAN
A. INTRODUCTION
1.    The Shandong Environment Project (SDEP) has four components, located in the
cities of Jinan, Weihai and Yantai and the XRB, which would involve land acquisition
and resettlement/rehabilitation of affected persons. To facilitate mitigation of the adverse
land acquisition-related impacts of the project and to comply with World Bank
requirements, the concerned implementing agency has prepared a comprehensive
Resettlement Action Plan (RAP) for each.
2.    This annex describes the land acquisition and resettlement impacts of the project
and discusses the frameworks developed by the Provincial and Municipal Governments
for implementing and financing land acquisition and resettlement activities. Section B
describes the land acquisition and social impacts of the project and the surveys carried out
to verify impacts and identify resettlement issues. Section C sets out the implementation
frameworks for Land Acquisition and Resettlement. Project implementation would
commence in 1997 and continue to 2001.
B. PROJECT IMPACTS
3.    The following project components would require land acquisition and/or
resettlement/rehabilitation of affected persons:
Jinan City
(a)   Jinan Wastewater Collection;
(b)   Jinan Yellow River Water Supply;
Weihai City
(c)   Weihai District Heating; and
Yantai City
(d)   Yantai District Heating.
One component includes resettlement that would be implemented under retroactively-
financed contracts; the Jinan Wastewater Collection Project which has five annual stages,
the first being implemented in 1996. The Weihai District Heating Project involved site



- 96 -                    ANNEX6
acquisition and resettlement by the Weihai City Government in 1993 as part of suburban
development. This would be implemented early in the project. In 1995, the site was
chosen for the proposed Weihai No. 3 District Heating Plant, which is to be constructed
as part of the project. Both of these components would have resettlement action plans
which were submitted for review at appraisal, and found satisfactory.
4.    Construction of the new  project components requires land acquisition,
resettlement of the affected persons, rehabilitation of businesses and livelihoods, and
temporary disturbances during construction. In order to verify the impact of project
components on the affected people and property, each implementing agency conducted a
census of affected persons and properties and a socioeconomic sample survey of affected
households, including attitudinal questions.
Land Acquisition and Social Impacts
5.    The data set out here represent the situation as of January 31, 1997. Most of the
project components have been approved to the feasibility or preliminary engineering
stages. The feasibility study approval sets the parameters for land acquisition, following
which the implementing agencies can notify local government and the affected people.
This enables detailed implementation planning and public consultation to proceed prior to
project construction.
6.    Land acquisition and social impacts are summarized as follows.
(a)   The amount of land to be permanently requisitioned is 992 hectares
(14,879 mu);
(b)   18 hectares (266 mu) of land would be affected temporarily during
construction;
(c)   300 hectares (4,500 mu) of farmland would be temporarily acquired after
2015 for sediment deposition. The land would be acquired gradually and
would be returned to farmland after deposition is complete;
(d)   667 households (2,120 people) need to have their housing relocated;
(e)   An estimated 4,574 farmers (including dependents) would have their
livelihoods affected by land acquisition;
(f)   Investments in new land, agricultural improvements, sideline activities and
enterprise employment is planned to restore livelihoods and incomes.
Most of the agricultural persons can remain in agriculture through land
redistribution, but some laborers may need to be provided with
nonagricultural enterprise jobs; and



- 97 -                     ANNEX6
(g)   The project would affect 306 workers in 82 shops, until the shops can be
relocated. The project would also affect 25 enterprises and 217 of their
staff. Most staff would retain the same jobs after reconstruction; the
remainder would be assisted in gaining new employment.
Table 1 summarizes impacts for the four project subcomponents.
7.    Detailed baseline socioeconomic surveys of the affected people have been
completed by a local institute (Shandong University in Jinan). The baseline survey
measures the income levels and standards of living of the affected persons before land
acquisition and resettlement. This baseline survey forms the basis for comparing their
income levels and standards of living as would be reported by the monitoring reports
during project implementation.
Minimization of Land Acquisition and Resettlement
8.    The feasibility studies included an analysis of land acquisition and possible
resettlement, as part of the identification of project options. While the selection between
options was based on technical and economic criteria, the potential impact on project
affected persons was also considered.
(a)   In Weihai, a project site was selected in the urban fringe which had
already been zoned for industrial development. Land acquisition and
resettlement had already been successfully completed by the city
government in 1993.
(b)   In Yantai, the construction of the steam/heating pipeline has been routed
next to roadways to avoid acquisition of housing.
(c)   In Jinan, the Wastewater Collection Project involves the construction of
new underground sewers. These can be constructed along the road surface
without affecting adjacent buildings. However, certain segments (12.4 km
out of 50.9 km to be constructed) are along narrow roads that the city has
planned to widen for better urban transport corridors and redevelopment of
old city blocks. For these segments, a RAP has been prepared.
(d)   The Jinan Yellow River Water Supply Project involves the construction of
a storage reservoir. The proposed site (Queshan Reservoir) is in a flood
detention zone, and therefore affects fewer people and less arable farmland
than any comparable sites near Jinan.



- 98 -                    ANNEX6
C. FRAMEWORK FOR IMPLEMENTATION OF LAND ACQUISITION AND RESETTLEMENT
ACTIVITIES
9.    Each RAP for the four project subcomponents with land acquisitions describes the
framework and action plans for implementing the land acquisition and resettlement
program. Each framework comprises the following elements:
(a)   Objectives and principles of land compensation and resettlement
(b)   Legal framework and resettlement policies
(c)   Resettlement institutions and agencies
(d)   Relocation of housing and compensation for personal assets
(e)   Compensation for lost income and plans for affected livelihoods
(f)   Costs and budget
(g)   Implementation schedule and arrangements for resettlement
(h)   Participation and consultation with affected persons
(i)   Redressal of grievance and appeals process
(j)    Monitoring and evaluation of RAP implementation.
10.   The four RAPs covering the above  components of the resettlement
implementation framework have been prepared by the implementing agencies based on
discussions with the concerned levels of government; the RAPs have been approved by
the concerned municipal authorities, and would be implemented as an integral part of the
project. The components of the framework for implementation are described below.
Objectives and Principles of Resettlement
11.   The main objective for each of the resettlement action plans is to ensure that the
standards of living and income levels of all categories of affected persons are improved,
or at least restored, after resettlement. The main principles for implementing the RAP are
as follows.
(a)   Minimize land acquisition and resettlement as much as possible through
choice of project components and changes in design and alignment.
(b)   Pay compensation for all structures at the replacement value of those
structures.
(c)   Pay compensation for collective land to the local collective.  This
compensation would be used to increase the productive capacity of the
collectives which in tum would rehabilitate incomes and livelihoods, and
would not be used for any other purpose.
(d)   Agencies may arrange some jobs in nonagricultural enterprises for some
persons whose lands are acquired. The majority of affected agricultural
people would remain in agricultural livelihoods.



-99 -                       ANNEX6
(e)   All infrastructure, such as municipal roads, communication, lighting,
sewerage, treatment work, etc. would be restored or replaced. The
concerned implementing agency would fully compensate for
reconstruction at replacement cost.
(f)    All affected enterprises would be compensated for any losses caused by
the project (i.e., disruption of their property) including related assets,
income and wages.
(g)   The implementing agencies would compensate persons affected by
temporary construction impacts to ensure their standards of living are not
adversely affected.
(h)   The implementing agencies would plan and carry out land acquisition and
resettlement activities with the participation of affected persons and their
representatives.
(i)    The affected persons or enterprises have avenues for redress of their
grievances and if they are not satisfied with the outcome, they can appeal
to higher authority and to civil courts.
(j)    For older people, special rehabilitation measures would be taken to ensure
that elderly affected persons are able to maintain their standards of living.
12.   The following guidelines and principles would be followed:
(a)   New houses of the affected persons would be of at least the same standard
as the affected houses or equal to the minimum current standards,
whichever is the greater;
(b)   All infrastructure affected would be restored or replaced at project cost; in
a manner which maintains or improves the quality of services;
(c)   All affected shops would be assisted by the implementing agencies to
resume operations at nearby locations;
(d)   Shop and enterprise workers would be provided with allowances
equivalent to their salaries for the period of closure; and
(e)   Planning and implementation of the RAPs would be carried out with the
participation of the affected persons and their representatives.
Legal Framework and Resettlement Policies
13.   The RAPs have been prepared based on the following laws, regulations and
guidelines for land acquisition and resettlement issued by various authorities in China:



-100 -                     ANNEX6
(a)   The People's Republic of China Land Management Law
(b)   The People's Republic of China Land Management Implementation Act
(c)   Rules of Land Acquisition for National Construction
(d)   Land Administration Implementation Measures of Shandong Province
(e)   Municipal regulations for land requisition and resettlement compensation.
14.   The implementation of land acquisition and resettlement program would be
carried out in accordance with the specific provisions of each subcomponent's RAP.
Compensation/Resettlement Policies for Different Categories of Project Impacts
15.   The following categories of project impacts were assessed during the census
survey of affected persons and properties:
(a)   Permanent land acquisition of collective land
(b)   Temporary disturbance/occupation of land during construction
(c)   Loss of housing
(d)   Households losing agricultural land and income
(e)   Relocation of shops and small businesses
(f)   Partial impacts on enterprises
(g)   Loss of standing crops
(h)   Indirect impacts in the disturbance zone.
16.   The RAPs provide full details of housing allocation and reconstruction options.
The compensation and/or resettlement policies for the above categories of impacts are as
follows.
(a)   Permanent Land Acquisition of Collective Land. The implementing
agency would pay compensation to the affected local government. The
rates have been provided in the RAP. The local governments would use
compensation payments to restore the productive capacity of the affected
villages through the development of agriculture-based and nonagriculture-
based economic activities.
(b)   Temporary Disturbance/Occupation of Land During Construction.
The implementing agency would compensate for temporary use based on
loss of net income from the land, including returns to labor. With this
compensation, the village officials would arrange the economic
rehabilitation and provide compensation directly to the persons
temporarily affected.
(c)   Loss of Housing.   Persons losing government-owned or private
apartments and houses would have the options of:
(i)    moving into a community-built house of the same standard as
previous house, without any extra payment; or



-101-                      ANNEX 6
(ii)   constructing a new house upon payment of compensation at
replacement value for the affected house or structure.  No
deductions would be made for depreciation.
(d)   Households Losing Agricultural Land and Income. All households
that lose income by land acquisition would be given the option of new
land, investment in other agricultural or sideline activities, or full-time
jobs in nonagricultural enterprises.
(e)   Relocation of Shops and Small Businesses. The implementing agency
and municipal land departments would assist business to find alternative
plots for continuing their businesses. Implementing agencies would
compensate such businesses for the replacement cost of the shop,
relocation costs and losses in net income during the period when their
business is closed.
(f)   Partial Impacts on Enterprises. The implementing agency would pay
the cost of restoration at full replacement value as compensation.
(g)   Loss of Standing Crops. Standing crops compensation would be paid
directly to the affected farmers at the full market value of comparable
mature crops at harvest.
(h)   Indirect Impacts in the Disturbance Zone. Indirect impacts due to the
construction of the project, on structures, businesses or people in the
disturbance zone would be compensated for physical loss at the full
Teplacement rates and/or net income loss. Structures needing repairs
would be compensated fully for the repairs. If any houses need to be
relocated due to such impact, they would be treated on the same lines as
houses affected by permanent land acquisition.
17.  In case it were found that the standards of living of any category of affected
persons have not been restored after relocation or resettlement, the implementing agency
would be responsible to take additional measures to ensure that the entitlements
stipulated by the RAP are achieved. Tables 2 to 5 summarize the categories of affected
people and their relevant entitlement, for each subcomponent.
Restoration of Household Incomes
18.   Most of the agricultural land to be acquired involves the Jinan Yellow River
Water Supply Project. Households (villages) that lose a small share of their household
incomes (less than 25 percent) can be allocated more land within the village and provided
compensation to improve production output on unaffected land or nonland-based income
generating activities. Households (villages) that lose most of their incomes (more than 75
percent) would be provided new land in a host village and some laborers would be
eligible for full-time nonagricultural jobs in enterprises. Households (villages) that lose a
significant share of their household incomes (25 to 75 percent) would restore livelihoods



-102-                     ANNEX6
and incomes through a combination of the above strategies. The income generation
schemes would guarantee that after resettlement household incomes are higher than
present income levels. During the transition period, food and income subsidies would be
provided by the implementing agency to maintain living standards. The transition period
would be minimized by proper scheduling of resettlement and project construction
activities. Job training would be provided for households that undertake new income-
generation activities. Also, such affected households would be eligible for preferential
policies that would be promulgated by the Jinan Municipal Government.
Resettlement Institutions and Agencies
19.   The Shandong Provincial Project Management Office (SPPMO) has vested
overall responsibility for coordination of planning and implementation of resettlement
activities with the project. SPPMO and the Municipal project offices in Jinan, Yantai and
Weihai are responsible for coordinating resettlement activities. The actual planning and
implementation of resettlement would be carried out by the borrower implementing
agencies, together with the township/district and village governments. In addition to
these, the main departments, bureaus and agencies responsible for various aspects of
resettlement planning, implementing, coordinating and monitoring resettlement activities
are the following:
(a)   The Planning Administration Office in each project city
(b)   The Shandong Provincial and Municipal Land administration Bureau for
Jinan, Yantai and Weihai
(c)   The Labor Bureau for Jinan, and Yantai
(d)   The Shandong Provincial and Municipal Urban and Rural Construction
Commissions for Jinan, Yantai and Weihai
(e)   Shandong Yellow River Management Office.
Costs and Budget
20.   The costs of RAP implementation is part of the overall budget of the project. The
annual requirement of funds would be included in the annual investment plan for the
project.  The base cost of land acquisition and resettlement is estimated to be
approximately Y 231.6 million ($27.9 million). The cost estimates by subcomponent are
shown in Table 6.
21.   The cost estimates would be adjusted based on the actual inflation rate. Physical
contingencies of 15 percent of the cost estimates have been budgeted. Each borrower
implementing agency is responsible for provision of adequate funds to carry out RAP
implementation. Cost overruns, if any, would be borne by each such agency.



-103-                      ANNEX6
Implementation Schedule
22.   The project subcomponents involving resettlement would be implemented
between 1996 and 2001, consistent with the overall project implementation schedule
shown in Anx 8. The RAPs have detailed implementation schedules and recommended
guidelines for project construction which minimize social impacts and enhance the
resettlement implementation program.  Assurances have been received from each
implementing agency that the RAP implementation would take precedence to the project
construction schedule in order to avoid unnecessary social impacts and related costs.
Participation and Consultations
23.   The RAPs have been developed with the active involvement of affected persons
and their representatives. The affected persons were informed about the project during or
after the feasibility studies were approved by the Municipal and Provincial Planning
Commissions. The affected persons and their representatives participated in the census
surveys carried out by each implementing agency and project office. Formal notifications
would be given to each affected household and business after the project is approved by
the Bank/IDA. The borrower agencies, assisted by local government have started to
inform the affected persons and households about the project components, the proposed
resettlement plans and the specific entitlements for each category of affected household
or business. The affected persons would participate in the following activities:
(a)   Selecting resettlement locations for residential sites and farmland;
(b)   Constructing their own houses (depending on their option);
(c)   Transferring their assets to the new houses;
(d)   Determining investment plans for restoration of incomes, production and
livelihoods;
(e)   Exercising the option of cash compensation for self-employment;
(f)   Recommending construction practices and schedules in order to minimize
income losses and social disturbances; and
(g)   Implementing  the  resettlement  program  through  their  village
representatives.
Resettlement Information Booklet
24.   Resettlement Information Booklets are being prepared for each project
component. The booklets would have a common section dealing with overall regulations
and principles and a section laying out the resettlement arrangements specific to each
affected area and category of impact. The booklet would be given to each affected person
to ensure adequate knowledge of their rights under the project and avenues of appeal.



-104-                      ANNEX6
Grievances and the Appeals Process
25.  In order to ensure that the affected persons have avenues for redressing their
grievances, detailed procedures for redressal of grievances have been established. Many
grievances can be handled by the implementing agency but if this not successful, an
affected person can initiate the formal process. First, the affected person could lodge a
grievance with the township Land Management Office, to which the Office should
respond within 15 days. Second, if the person is not satisfied, an appeal would be made
to a department within the District Government within a specified time. Third, if still
unsatisfied, the affected person could appeal to a department within the Municipal
Government for redressing the grievance. Finally, an appeal can be lodged with the
People's court within 15 days of receiving the decision from the Municipal level. The
grievance could be for any aspect of the RAP program, including rates of compensation.
Grievance mechanisms would be included in the Resettlement Information Booklet.
Monitoring and Evaluation
26.  In order to ensure that the implementation of resettlement would be in accordance
with the provisions of the RAPs, detailed monitoring and evaluation, both internal and
external, would be carried out during and after implementation of the project.
(a)   Internal Monitoring.  Each implementing agency is responsible for
internal monitoring of the RAP. This would be coordinated by SPPMO.
Monitoring would be carried out based on the provisions of the RAP. The
main indicators to be monitored are payment of compensation, allotment
of housing, restoration of livelihoods, allowances for workers lost wages,
relocation of shops, and restoration of enterprises.  The internal
monitoring reports would be prepared every three months for submission
to SPPMO. Preliminary monitoring formats have been prepared. An
internal monitoring report would be submitted to the Bank/IDA every six
months. The first internal monitoring report would be submitted on
March 31, 1997.
(b)   External Monitoring.  Independent institutes specializing in social
assessment would be contracted to conduct external monitoring and
evaluation of the RAPs. In addition to verifying the results of the internal
monitoring reports, these institutes would monitor the following
performance indicators: (i) timeliness and adequacy of compensation
payments; (ii) land acquisition and relocation; (iii) timely restoration of
livelihoods and income-generation activities; (iv) adequate restoration of
infrastructure; (v) restoration of shops and enterprises; (vi) provision of
housing; (vii) levels of satisfaction among the affected persons;
(viii) income levels and standards of living after relocation; (ix) level of
public participation; (x) effectiveness of resettlement management;
(xi) redressal of grievances; and (xii) follow-up for problem resolution.



-105-                     ANNEX6
27.   The External Monitoring and Evaluation Reports would be prepared by these
institutes every six months, until construction of all subcomponents is completed. The
first external monitoring report would be submitted to the Bank/IDA on June 30, 1997.
The report would review the adequacy of implementation measures and report on the
perceptions of the affected persons.  It would also identify critical issues in
implementation and make recommendations to improve overall effectiveness of the
resettlement program. The report would also report progress of follow-up action that was
to be initiated on the recommendations of previous monitoring reports. The agency
responsible for each subcomponent would negotiate a contract for external monitoring.
Two acceptable candidates include Shandong University and the Shandong Academy of
Social Sciences.
