ANNEX 15: ECONOMIC JUSTIFICATION
A. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
1.    This project responds to Bank sector work and experience and CAS objectives in
two ways: first through a comprehensive approach to environmental degradation in a
river basin, where it supports the rationale design and prioritization of interventions based
on cost-effectiveness considerations, improvements in the regulatory environment,
measurable reduction of current air and water pollution loads and protection of critical
groundwater resources; second, through establishment of financially sustainable utilities,
backed by a cost-recovering tariff policy. The objectives of the project are consistent
with China's Environmental Action Plan and Agenda 21, which provides the basis for
joint Government and Bank action in the environmental sector, especially in regard to the
urban environment. Finally, the project responds to the specific priorities expressed at
the provincial level, recognizing the effective decentralization of regulatory and
investment authority in China, while building on the local political commitment and
power to induce wider use of the solutions demonstrated under the project.
2.    The proposed project, the specific components of which are described in Annex 2,
would support critical components of a broad provincial program to implement a
comprehensive river basin environmental improvement program for the Xiaoqing River
together with a parallel improvement in urban air quality.
B. ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
3.    Shandong's proactive approach to environmental management responds to the
increasing economic costs caused by urban pollution.  These costs have become
increasingly visible as they begin to translate into financial losses of whole sectors, such
as the disappearance of aquaculture in the Xiaoqing River estuary, which represents an
approximate annual loss of Y 200 million. However, most of the environmental damages
have not yet been quantified, such as the damages to human health from air pollution,
losses in agriculture due to polluted irrigation water, depletion of groundwater resources,
and loss of cultural values such as the springs in Jinan.
4.    The large cost of a comprehensive program to reduce pollution in a whole river
basin requires carefal prioritization of interventions to maximize the net benefit from
these investments. Since environmental benefits can only partially be translated into
monetary values, the choice of priority interventions for this project had to be guided by
cost-effectiveness considerations, i.e., the least-cost approach to achieve an environmental
improvement. The project successfully identified several opportunities for cost-effective
interventions by making better use of existing but underutilized urban infrastructure and
by undertaking the critical complementary investments that are required for their efficient



-162 -                     ANNEX 15
operation: e.g., the water supply component in Jinan would allow the existing water
treatment plant to increase capacity utilization from less than 25 percent to about 100
percent. The environmental subloans to industry, in turn, would focus on cost-effective
interventions of pollution prevention and waste minimization, thereby reducing the need
for more costly end-of-pipe investments.
5.   It became clear during project identification that Shandong and its cities needed
substantial change in policy and institutional structures. Policy problems included urban
environmental services priced too low to discourage waste generation or to provide the
financing needed to build, maintain and operate services such as district heating or
wastewater treatment. Therefore, investment choices were made with a view toward
maximizing the impact of policy and institutional innovation within the province by
supporting the establishment of incorporated public utilities, such as the Jinan Drainage
company, or the design of tariff policies that allow financial sustainability of operations.
City and Sector Choices
6.    After reviewing potential investments in several provincial cities, four were
selected for further project development. Jinan, the capital of Shandong Province, which
ranks fourth among the 37 "environmentally most stressed cities in China" according to a
NEPA pollution index, was selected because of its major environmental problems, and its
immediate impact on the water quality of the Xiaoqing River. The other two cities,
Yantai and Weihai, are medium-size cities that had a clearly defined strategy to address
their air pollution problems through the expansion of district heating and offered
opportunities to demonstrate new technologies that would be relevant to other similarly
sized cities in the province. Priority investments in industrial pollution reduction were
identified based on a ranking of the strongest polluting sectors, i.e., paper and pulp,
breweries, and chemical industry, and within these sectors, based on opportunities for
cost-effective and innovative interventions that would provide useful experiences to other
industries in the same sector.
Water Supply in Jinan
7.    Jinan's population of 1.5 million currently consumes about 0.9 million tons/day of
water, which is supplied from three major sources: (a) groundwater (85 percent);
(b) surface water from the Yellow River (10 percent), and (c) reservoirs (5 percent).
Jinan has relied on groundwater to meet its increasing water needs over the last decades.
Today, industry extracts 28 percent and the Water Supply Company the remaining 72
percent of the estimated total 880,000 tons/day of groundwater.
8.    Current Problems. The current volume of groundwater extraction has become
unsustainable. The average level of groundwater above sea level has dropped from
almost 30 m in 1960 to 27.4 m in 1995. The seasonal fluctuations of the groundwater
level have increased from less then 1 m to over 3.9 m during the same period. These
developments indicate an unsustainable level of extraction.  The Water Resource
Conservation Office sets the sustainable volume of extraction at 500,000 tons/day, based
on an estimated daily replenishment of 950,000 tons/day, a spring flow of 250,000



-163 -                     ANNEX 15
tons/day and 200,000 tons/day for agricultural extraction and evaporation. A visible
indicator for groundwater depletion is the reduced flow of Jinan's famous springs.
Reduced spring flow decreased the flow of surface water within Jinan and to the
Xiaoqing River, and, as a result, led to a higher concentration of pollution from industrial
and domestic effluents, specifically during the dry season. This pollution poses a risk to
the quality of the groundwater in Jinan and near the Xiaoqing River.
9.    The Government has introduced regulations to control groundwater extraction,
including quota, individual metering, and charges but has not been able to impose
reductions of groundwater use for lack of viable alternative water sources.
10.   Utilization of surface water from the Yellow River water has been severely
constrained by limited treatment capacity and seasonal fluctuations of the Yellow River
flow. Water extraction started in the 1980s with the construction of a diversion channel
and a 100,000 tons/day pretreatment plant (No. 1), which was later on complemented
with a second much larger 400,000 tons/day water treatment plant. However, limited
capacity of the newer plant to cope with the high level of silt required a pretreatment in
the older facility. As a result, actual water supply from the Yellow River was limited to
100,000 tons/day. Actual water supply was further reduced due to seasonal low flow or
even stoppage of the Yellow River (79 days in 1995, 29 days in 1994) which left the
plant without raw water. As a result, Jinan experiences seasonal shortages of water; the
shortfall is estimated to amount to up to 100,000 tons/day. The shortfall affects primarily
industry and residential areas in the elevated parts of the city, which suffers from low
pressure during these periods.
11.   Business-as-Usual Scenario. Water demand is projected to increase 21 percent
by the year 2000 and over 70 percent by 2005. A review of the water demand projections
confirmed the earlier findings that Jinan faces an increasing shortfall in water supply.
Industrial water demand is projected to increase from about 650,000 m 3/day in 1996 to
950,000 in 2010. These projections are already based on an ambitious assumption of an
annual 6 percent decline of the water consumption per unit of industrial output,
decreasing from 6,000 m3/Y million in 1995 to 2,500 m3/Y million in 2010. Jinan has a
track record of already having achieved, between 1990 and 1995, a reduction from
15,000 m3/Y million to 6,000 (or 20 percent per year). Nonetheless, achieving such
sustained water use efficiency would require special attention to and incentives for water
conservation measures in industries. For private households, increasing water tariffs are
likely to encourage water conservation: a price elasticity of -0.17 for domestic per capita
water consumption was estimated based on JWSC's last 15 years' sales. However, rising
incomes and living standards would more than compensate this impact with an estimated
per capita income elasticity of 0.22. As a result, aggregate demand by households
(including public buildings) at a population growth rate of 2.3 percent (1.9 percent after
2000) would increase from about 300,000 m3/day in 1995 to over 650,000 m3/day in
2010.
12.   If no additional water resources become available, the shortfall of water supply
would rise to 400,000 tons/day (by 2000) and 860,000 tons/day (by 2010), respectively.



-164-                       ANNEX 15
Groundwater would continue to be extracted at unsustainable levels, and seasonal
fluctuations would be accentuated. Water shortages would hamper Jinan's industrial
development, which currently absorbs 70 percent of the water, and quality of life and
health of urban residents would be affected, at a time when expectations toward standards
of living are rising in parallel with higher incomes. If, on the other hand, additional
groundwater would be used to meet the shortfall, its depletion would be accelerated and
the mentioned problems exacerbated.
13.   Long-Term Objective. Hence, Jinan developed a long-term strategy to protect
its groundwater while meeting the rising demand for water by increasing the share of
water supplied from surface water. The target is to increase the surface water share to
about 45 percent in 2000 and to over 60 percent in 2010. The first step to achieve these
targets is to increase actual water extraction from the Yellow River from less than
100,000 to 400,000 tons/day, the design capacity of the second treatment plant. The next
step would be the upgrading of the first treatment plant to supply 100,000 tons/day of
treated water. The third step after the year 2000 would be the implementation of the
Tianshan scheme. The proposed project would support the first phase of this long-term
program through the construction of (a) a settlement basin to desilt the raw water and
(b) a regulating reservoir to compensate for the seasonal fluctuations of the Yellow River
flow.
14.   Economic Analysis. The proposed project generates environmental benefits and
improved urban services that cannot be valued directly.  Several approaches have
therefore been applied to assess the economic soundness and the net benefits of the
project: first, a least-cost analysis of water supply is undertaken, second, a cost-benefit
analysis is conducted using willingness to pay and opportunity costs as revealed by recent
tariffs as a proxy for benefits; and third, intangible and unquantifiable benefits are being
described.
15.   Least-Cost Solution. The following two options were compared to determine the
least-cost solution to meet Jinan's additional 400,000 tons/day water supply by the year
2000: (a) import water from Tianshan over 60 km away to a new water treatment plant in
Dayang in a western suburb of Jinan; and (b) build the Queshan reservoir and settling
pond and connect them to the existing Yellow River Water Treatment Plant No. 2, i.e.,
the project that has been proposed for Bank funding.
16.   Option (b), or the proposed project, is the more cost-effective solution. First, it
makes optimal use of existing but underutilized infrastructure, in particular the Yellow
River Water Treatment Plant.
17.   Second, by comparison, the proposed Tianshan scheme would construct a new
water treatment facility, a new canal of about 60 km and a new extraction gate and would
connect to a multipurpose reservoir that still needs to be built. As a result, for the same
amount of water supply, the total project cost for the water supply component alone
would amount to Y 600 million, not even including the estimated Y 500 to 700 million
for construction of the dam. The project costs would be 58 percent higher than in the



-165 -                     ANNEX 15
proposed Queshan project and the unit cost per ton of treated water per day would be 11
percent higher.
18.   Cost-Benefit Analysis. The economic net benefit of the proposed project was
estimated based on the economic benefits derived from water supply, only, and adjusted
project costs. The environmental and other benefits are being dealt with in subsequent
sections.
19.   Economic Benefits. The project generates benefits in form of incremental water
supplied to consumers. Since water is distributed to different consumer groups, i.e.,
residential, industrial, and commercial, the economic benefits have been estimated
separately for each group. The relative shares of water consumption are expected to
remain constant, with an estimated 33 percent supplied to households and 52 percent
supplied to industry and commerce, and about 15 percent of unaccounted-for water for
other uses including system losses such as leakages.
20.   Ideally, economic benefits for households are determined based on willingness-to-
pay studies. Such studies have been undertaken for other cities (e.g., Kunming) but are
not available for Jinan. Another approach to determine the benefit is the cost of
alternative water supply, or the opportunity cost. However, no alternative suppliers, such
as private vendors could be identified in Jinan, and individual households are not allowed
to extract groundwater. In the case of serious shortages, the municipality trucks in water
and supplies it free of charge. Hence, no parallel market for water has developed.
Therefore, the existing total water charge of Y 0.66/ton was used as an approximation to
the willingness to pay. That charge in 1996 was composed of Y 0.5/ton for the water
tariff proper, Y 0.1 for a municipal water supply fund, and Y 0.06 for utility taxes. The
1995 tariff of is comparable to the findings of the willingness-to-pay studies in Kunming,
which indicated a range of Y 0.60 to 0.72/ton, and to water prices in neighbor cities of
Shandong (about Y 0.70/ton). Therefore, the current total charge has been used to
calculate the economic benefits to households.
21.   The economic benefits to industry have been estimated in two ways: through the
opportunity cost of self-extracted groundwater and the water charge. Currently, 299
industries hold permits to extract up to 250,000 tons/day at an average cost of Y 1.1/ton.
This cost is composed of extraction costs of about Y 0.40/ton, including depreciation and
operation, and a Y 0.70/ton water resource fee to be paid to the municipal Water
Conservation Office. Theoretically, this cost can be considered an opportunity cost that
industry would incur in case no water would be available through the Water Supply
Company. This cost is comparable to the current water charge of Y 1.1 0/ton, including a
surcharge for the municipal water supply fund of Y 0.20/ton and the 10 percent utility
tax, the total price of water for industry adds up to Y 1. 1/ton. Therefore, the total water
charge has been used as a proxy for industry's benefit from water supply. In a similar
fashion, the total water charge of Y 1.65/ton was used to estimate benefits to the
commercial sector.



-166 -                       ANNEX 15
22.   Economic Costs and Net Economic Benefits. Economic cost estimates were
based on adjusted financial costs provided by the financial analysis of the Jinan Water
Supply Company. The net economic benefit of the proposed project is then Y 150
million, discounted at 12 percent over a lifetime of 20 years and the corresponding ERR
is 18 percent. This result is robust with regard to cost overruns of investments and
operation: assuming a 20 percent higher cost, the corresponding NPV is still positive at
Y 50 million and the ERR is 14 percent. The cost of the existing, but underutilized water
treatment plant was excluded-as a sunk cost-in the baseline scenario. However, to
ensure the economic viability of the combined investment, the estimated residual value of
the existing plant was included in the "Sunk Cost Included" scenario: even then the net
present value (NPV) remains positive at Y 120 million and the ERR is 17 percent.
23.   Unquantifiable and Intangible Benefits.  In addition to the tangible and
quantifiable benefits provided to water users, the project also generates environmental
benefits in terms of groundwater protection and conservation of cultural heritage, which
are more difficult to value.
TABLE 1: JINAN WATER SUPPLY: ECONOMIC ANALYSIS

La High-Cost Scenario: 20 percent cost overrun of investment and operational cost.
Lb Sunk-Cost Scenario: including as additional cost the in-use value of the existing treatment plant of
Y 42.5 million, equivalent to 50 percent of the original investment of about Y 85 million.
24.   The proposed project would not only satisfy the increasing water demand of Jinan
in the year 2000 but, in addition, allow for a reduction of groundwater extraction by about
10 percent, or 70,000 tons/day. It therefore contributes to Jinan's strategic objective of
reduced reliance on groundwater. By contrast, if the additional volume of water were to
be supplied from groundwater, overall extraction in the year 2000 would reach about 1.2
million tons/day, more than double the recommended sustainable extraction rate of
500,000 tons/day. Overextraction could lead to irreversible damages due to accelerated
infiltration of polluted surface water, given the limestone characteristics of Jinan's
aquifers. In addition, it is doubtful that such overextraction would be feasible because
neighboring communities are likely to refuse Jinan Municipality access to their
groundwater resources in order to meet the growing demand in their own communities.
25.   The project would also have an intangible benefit in form of contributing to the
preservation of the cultural heritage: preserving the famous springs in Jinan. There are
also some tangible but unquantified benefits in terms of avoided loss in tourism.



-167 -                      ANNEX 15
Preservation of cultural values and the long-term environmental benefits add to the
overall net benefit of the project.
Jinan Wastewater
26.   Jinan currently generates daily about 680,000 tons/day wastewater, which is
discharged without treatment either directly or through sewers to the Xiaoqing River. A
wastewater treatment facility was built and completed in 1994 but is presently not
operating because the existing sewer system is deficient and a new intercepting sewer
needs to be constructed to supply the plant with wastewater.
27.   Current Problems. As a result, the water quality in watercourses and lakes
within the city and the Xiaoqing River has deteriorated dramatically. Within the city,
water quality has reached a level that exceeds the lowest National Surface Water Class V
standards. This has turned most of the city's rivers and lakes into open sewers that emit
odors and can cause health problems, which so far have not been quantified for Jinan. In
the Xiaoqing River, a COD level of 220 mg/l has been measured, exceeding the Class V
standards of 25 mg/l over seven times. Since there are no major upstream urban centers,
Jinan is largely responsible for the water quality in the Jinan reach of the Xiaoqing River.
The Government of Jinan is also concerned that the lack of effective sewage treatment
facilities may deter new investors, such as international joint ventures.
28.   Further downstream, Jinan's water pollution has contributed to the deterioration
of water quality, which has already imposed tangible losses on agriculture, aquaculture,
fishery and groundwater quality. Reportedly, about 100,000 mu of aquaculture in the
Xiaoqing River estuary were wiped out during the last six years because of river
pollution, representing an estimated loss of over Y 200 million ($25 million) per year.'
Fishery in the river stopped about 10 years ago due to pollution and dwindling quantities.
Today, the remaining fishing fleet in the estuary has to travel for about 10 hours to areas
at least 40 km off the coast to catch fish, greatly reducing the productivity and income of
fishermen. Groundwater pollution near the Xiaoqing River made it necessary to close
wells within 500 to 1,500 m of the Xiaoqing River and to rebuild them further away from
the river. It appears that Xiaoqing River water is still being used for irrigation, although
at much reduced levels because the quality of the products seemed to be affected.
29.   Business-as-Usual Scenario. If no action is taken and the current situation is
allowed to continue environmental damages and economic costs would increase. In line
with increased water consumption, the wastewater stream in 2000 and 2010 is expected to
have increased by 21 and 73 percent compared to 1995, and so would the pollution load.
Overall, Jinan would not make any progress toward achieving the Class V target water
quality for the city and would in part offset Shandong Province's concerted effort of
cleaning up the Xiaoqing River. In addition to the environmental concerns, the lack of an
1 Estimate based on interviews in Weifang Prefecture using the following assumptions: annual shrimp
production per mu: 100 kg, price per kg: Y 100; annual revenue per mu: Y 10,000. Assuming a value
added of Y 20 ($2.50), the total forgone value added amounts to Y 200,000,000 or $25 million.



-168 -                      ANNEX 15
operating sewerage system would seriously worsen the quality of urban life at a time
when expectations of the population for improved urban services is rising in parallel with
growing incomes and living standards.
30.   Long-Term Objective. Jinan has therefore prepared a long-term wastewater
management plan to reduce pollution from wastewater and to meet the urban sewerage
needs. The plan foresees investments of over Y 1 billion in an extended sewer network
and additional treatment capacity. As a first step, Jinan built the Sewage Treatment Plant
(STP) for about Y 305 million, which is currently idle. The proposed project would
provide the missing connection to the sewerage network and enhance its collection
capacity to allow the STP to operate at its full capacity of 220,000 tons/day, or about 45
percent of the total waste stream. After 2001, a second phase of this STP would be built,
expanding its capacity to 450,000 tons/day. Meanwhile, excess flows would continue to
spill over into the Xiaoqing River.
31.   Economic Analysis. Ideally, a full cost and benefit analysis should be used to
assess the economic net benefit of the proposed investment. However, the quantification
of the economic benefits, which are largely environmental and health improvements,
poses challenging analytical problems, exacerbated by the lack of local data. While there
is no doubt that water pollution results in tangible downstream damage costs, attributing
any specific amount of avoided damage cost to Jinan's wastewater treatment based on
available information would be arbitrary. Under the project, technical assistance to
develop a river model is being financed, which would provide a better understanding of
the economics of the river basin cleanup effort. Therefore, at this point, the economic
analysis focuses on a description of the benefits, the costs to achieve these benefits, and
some of the risks involved.
32.   Economic Benefits. Environmental benefits of undertaking the project include:
(a) a 20 percent reduction of Jinan's COD pollution load discharged into the Xiaoqing
River, which is expected to reduce COD concentration in the river by about 45 percent to
130 mg/l; since this level is still more than five times the target standard for Class V
water (25 mg/i), only marginal short-term benefits for downstream water users, such as
aquaculture or agriculture can be expected. Meaningful benefits would only result from
sustained efforts to reduce pollution from all sources; (b) reduced pollution of the inner
city water bodies, in particular the Daming Lake, which is currently seriously polluted by
direct discharges from industries; and (c) reduced groundwater contamination in Jinan
where groundwater is very close to the surface, supplying the city's famous springs, and
downstream, where many communities had to abandon groundwater wells that were
within 500 to 1,500 m of the Xiaoqing River.
33.  In addition, the project would improve sewerage services to a population of about
1 million and reduce health and safety hazards related to sewage overflows in areas where
currently sewer lines are clogged or connected to the drainage pipes. Such stoppages
would be eliminated through larger sewer capacity and improved design, specifically of
the inverted siphons.



-169-                       ANNEX 15
34.   A long-term benefit of the project is the support for the startup of an independent
and financially viable drainage company. The Jinan Drainage Company was formally
created in September 1996 and would operate as an autonomous entity with financial
responsibility for covering its operational cost and debt service and to contribute an
increasing amount to its capital investments required to keep expanding its services and
to meet the growing future demand. Through its full cost-recovery tariff policy, the
utility would internalize, at least partially, the cost of wastewater pollution abatement
costs and pass it on to water users.
35.   Economic Costs. To achieve these benefits, Jinan would invest Y 264 million in
an improved sewerage network and a connection to the existing STP, which could then
reach its full capacity of 80 million tons/year. Incremental operating costs would be
small because the STP is already fully staffed and operated for maintenance. By contrast,
without the project, Jinan already incurs annual cost of approximately Y 15 million for
depreciation and maintenance of the idle STP without achieving any environmental or
other benefits. This estimate is based on the cost of the 160 staff currently employed in
the plant and an annual depreciation of 4.5 percent of the Y 305 million investment.
36.   An alternative to the project could be the construction of smaller decentralized
treatment plants. However, this option would not be economical given the existing
underutilized treatment capacity in Jinan's central treatment facility. Even without the
existing facility, the high density of population with relatively lower network costs would
favor a central facility.
37.   Operational risks related to industrial effluents need to be managed. About 70
percent of the wastewater flow comes from industry. Industry would benefit from being
allowed to discharge effluents with COD levels of up to 500 mg/l while the effluent
standard for direct discharges is up to 300 mg/l. The corresponding limits for biological
oxygen demand (BOD) are 260 mg/l and for TSP 400 mg/l. There is a risk that industries
would not adequately pretreat their effluents and exceed the allowed limits if the
monitoring and enforcement of such standards is not improved. This is particularly true
for heavy metal and other pollutants which are prohibited from being discharged into
municipal sewerage systems at levels that could disrupt the treatment process.
Effectively controlling such effluents would require the collaboration between the
environmental agencies and the utility. Such arrangements would be further elaborated in
the context of the institutional strengthening of utilities.
District Heating
38.   Use of coal for heating in residential, public and commercial property is a major
source of SO2 and particle pollution of the local air in Shandong Province and has
contributed to the deterioration of air quality, which, particularly during the heating
season, falls well below the prescribed national standards: for example, in the capital
city, the daily average concentration of SO2 during the heating season was 19 times
higher than during the nonheating season and exceeded the SO2 ambient air quality
standard class III. The major sources of heat supply are coal-based (a) stoves with a low



-170-                       ANNEX15
thermal efficiency of only 25 percent, (b) small boilers with an average efficiency of 50
percent, and (c) district heating with larger and more efficient boilers. District heating
currently covers typically about 20 percent of urban heat demand.
39.   Business-as-Usual Scenario. If current heating patterns remained unchanged,
coal consumption for heating would have to increase to meet growing residential and
industrial heat demand. As a result, S02 and TSP levels would rise further. For example,
between 1996 and 2000, coal consumption in Weihai would increase by 67 percent, while
the daily average concentration of S02 would double. Increased levels of air pollution
have a negative impact on human health, causing respiratory problems that can lead to
chronic illness and a loss in labor productivity. Coal burning also causes CO2 emissions,
which have a negative impact on the global environment by contributing to global
warming.
40.   Long-Term Objective. The Government, therefore, for a long time has been
pursuing the introduction of less-polluting systems of local heat supply as a top
environmental priority.
41.   It is in this context, that the Government of Shandong Province selected Yantai
and Weihai as project sites to expand district heating. These choices are well justified:
Yantai is a medium-size city that offers the opportunity to convert an existing power
plant into a CHP, thereby increasing the combined efficiencies of heat and power
generation. Weihai is another medium-size city that offers the opportunity to set up a
new CHP based on an innovative fluidized-bed coal-combustion technology. The plant
would serve the fast-expanding industrial and residential district in the south, thereby
preventing the spread of less-efficient small boilers to serve industrial process heat and
residential heating needs. Both sites explore different district heating solutions and
would therefore provide useful lessons for other cities in the province.
42.   The air quality policies of the provincial and local governments further support
the introduction and expansion of district heating systems. In general, air quality
standards in Shandong Province are stricter than at the national level. Desulfurization
equipment is required for large boilers that bum coal with a sulfur content above 1
percent. Regulations have been passed that forbid the future installation of small boilers
below 10 tons/ hour in city areas. They also require the installation of central heating in
new buildings.  In areas with district heating, the connection to such systems is
compulsory.
43.   Economic Analysis. Ideally, a full cost and benefit analysis, including the value
of avoided air pollution damages, should be used to evaluate each district heating
proposal. However, without reliable local data on the cost of health and other damages
caused by air pollution, such analysis is difficult to undertake. Therefore, the following
three approaches were chosen for the economic analysis: (a) least-cost analysis of the
district heating projects compared to the two major options in terms of heat generation
costs; (b) comparison of their environmental impact, including coal consumption



-171-                       ANNEX 15
emissions of S02, ITSP, and C02; and (c) description of other intangible economic costs
and benefits.
44.   Least-Cost Option. The following three options were selected to determine the
least-cost option for meeting the estimated future demand of residential heat, industrial
steam, and cooling in the project area by the year 2000: (a) business as usual, providing
heat through a combination of small boilers and stoves, as today; all incremental heat
(1996 to 2000) would be supplied by new small boilers since no new stoves are allowed;
(b) small boilers only, replacing all existing stoves with small boilers and continued use
of the existing small boilers; and (c) district heating, replacing all existing stoves and
small boilers, i.e., the proposed project.
45.   Two other options were considered but rejected because they would not be
available in the project areas: (a) the use of natural gas, which would be beneficial from
an environmental perspective, but the natural gas transmission and distribution system for
these cities is not in place, and sufficient natural gas is not available within the time
horizon of the project; and (b) the use of oil-fired boilers, which would facilitate
environmental improvements with small boilers; however, current Government policy
prohibits the use of oil for general heating for balance-of-payment reasons (China is a net
importer of oil). But even if the Government would remove this restriction, coal-based
district heating would be the preferred option, given the high load densities in the project
area that keep network cost low and given the higher cost of nonsulfurized fuel oil that
would be needed to achieve the same environmental standard as with district heating.
46.   Results for Yantai. With the full introduction of district heating in Yantai,
64,000 household stoves and 200 small boilers would have been replaced by the year
2000. A comparison of the economic cost of district heating with "business as usual"
(option 1) and the "small boiler option" (option 2) over a 20-year time horizon indicated
savings under the district heating option of Y 0.2 billion if compared to the first and Y 0.5
billion if compared to the second option. The respective ERR on these net savings are 24
and 66 percent. These net savings are, to a large extent, the result of increases in fuel
efficiency, i.e., lower coal consumption: under the District Heating option, coal
consumption drops by 50 and 30 percent, respectively, compared to Options 1 and 2.
Investment costs are lower, too, even without accounting for the net gain from land
reclamation resulting from the elimination of small boiler houses. However, since land
prices are not firmly established, there is uncertainty regarding the actual market value.
47.   The economic costs of the proposed Yantai system differ sharply from those of
the financial analysis, since the power generating units that would be converted to CHP
production already exist and are treated as "sunk" costs. The only additional expenses
incurred are the connecting costs to the new district heating network and the costs of
forgone power production during the heating season plus the house connection costs from
the heat substations to individual living quarters. However, economic benefits of district
heating are robust; if a city, with a population density similar to Yantai, had no CHP
plant, and a new plant with a distribution network had to be constructed on a "green
field" basis, the economic analysis of this case shows only a small reduction in the ERR



-172-                       ANNEX 15
of less than 2 percent. The small difference can be explained by the slightly more
efficient power generation in large condenser plants compared to CHP plants. For the
two alternatives, stoves plus small boilers (option 1) and small boilers only, a significant
portion of overall costs is accounted for by the replacement costs for worn-out equipment.
A copy of the full economic analysis of the three alternative options can be found in the
project files. Table 2 provides a summary of the results of the above analysis.

48.   Results for Weihai. Introduction of district heating in Weihai's Economic
Development District would lead to the replacement of over 18,000 stoves and about 100
small boilers by the year 2000. Savings under the district heating option amount to
Y 0.35 billion if compared to the first and over Y 0.5 billion if compared to the second
option. The respective ERR on these net savings are 28 and 41 percent. Coal
consumption under the District Heating option drops by 40 and 35 percent compared to
Options 1 and 2. The savings from land that becomes available under the district heating
option have been excluded from the calculation. On the other hand, for the purpose of
comparison it was assumed that stoves would generate a similar heat comfort as district
and central heating, i.e., approximately 18C constant room temperature during the
heating season. However in 1995, Weihai stove owners actually heated much less and
typically had room temperatures of 10 to 12C during four hours a day only, thereby
consuming on average only a third of the coal required for constant 18C room
temperature. Even if compared against the low stove temperature scenario, the district
heating option is still more economical.
49.   In Weihai, the majority of the heat production would be sold as industrial steam
for production, heating and cooling. It could be argued that without district heating
industries would improve the efficiency of their small boilers over time. But even
assuming an increase of average small boiler efficiency from 50 to 65 percent, district
heating would still remain the more economical solution for Weihai's Economic
Development District. Table 3 provides a summary of these results.

50.    Environmental Benefits.  In addition to saving costs in heat generation, the
district heating option also generates environmental benefits. Introducing district heating
would reduce S02, TSP, and CO2 emissions as a result of two factors: (a) higher fuel
efficiency, i.e., lower coal consumption and (b) more effective and better-managed air
pollution control devices. Reduced emissions, together with the effect of higher stacks,
would then lead to improved ambient air quality in both Yantai and Weihai.
51.    In Yantai, district heating would lead to a 97 percent reduction of S02 and 96
percent reduction of TSP emissions if compared to the "business-as-usual" option, i.e.,
stoves and boilers. Compared to the small boiler option, the reductions are 96 percent for
SO2 and 90 percent for TSP. These results are summarized in Table 4.
52.    In Weihai, district heating would lead to a 80 percent reduction of SO2 and 90
percent reduction of TSP emissions if compared to the "business-as-usual" option, i.e.,
stoves and boilers. Compared to the small boiler option, the reductions are 80 percent for
SO2 and 85 percent for TSP. These results are summarized in Table 5.

53.    In order to assess the full cost of each option, the economic cost of air pollution
should be included. However, the methodologies to calculate the economic cost of air
pollution are still being debated and reliable data on health impact and exposure are not



-175 -                        ANNEX 15
available in Shandong. Some information on health costs related to air pollution is
available internationally, but this information needs to be adjusted to local circumstances.
In order to illustrate the impact of benefits from reduced air pollution damage on the
overall economic benefits, a simplified approach (so-called benefit-transfer approach) has
been applied, using data from Europe (the United Kingdom) adjusted by the ratio of GNP
in China and the United Kingdom. Based on this approach, the following benefits per ton
removed were applied: Y 440 for SO2, Y 3,800 for TSP, and Y 1.6 for CO2. As a result,
project costs increased by 15 percent to 50 percent for the various options. Considering
pollution cost makes district heating economically even more attractive: The district
heating option generates additional savings of Y 130 million in the form of reduced
emissions (or Y 90 million if compared to option 2). TSP reductions represent the largest
share (over 80 percent) of these savings, followed by SO2 (about 15 percent) and CO2
(less than 5 percent). Including the cost of pollution does not change the ranking of
options in this case, but points to additional benefits of the project for the local
enviromnent (SO2, TSP) and the global environment (CO2 reduction) (see Table 6).

54.   Other Costs and Benefits. The height of stacks of the two district heating plants
is over 100 m, allowing for a better dispersion of emissions. By comparison, the stack
height of existing small boilers is typically 20 to 35 m. Occasionally, the stack height is
lower than the surrounding buildings, exposing nearby dwellers directly to air pollution.
District heating would also significantly reduce exposure to indoor pollution: stoves
exhaust fumes through windows or building chimneys and require handling of coal
within the building, causing air pollution within the building where exposure to its
residents is greatest.
55.   As part of the district heating projects, tariff adjustments are being made to ensure
a.full cost recovery of the heating operation. These costs include the investment and the



-176 -                     ANNEX 15
operation of effective pollution control equipment. Therefore, these district heating
projects effectively internalize the cost of pollution abatement, which in turn provides an
incentive to use heat in an efficient manner.
56.   While, in the aggregate, consumer would face the full cost of heating, including
environmental pollution control, this price signal is currently not being passed on directly
to the individual end-user since residential central heating bills are typically based on
floor area, but not on actual heat consumption. There is therefore a risk that when
moving from individual stoves (where the cost is directly linked to consumption) to
central heating, people would consume heat in a more wasteful manner. The project
therefore promotes the introduction of individual metering for each household unit. A
demonstration project would be implemented in Yantai that would test individual
metering and billing procedures. Also, assurance would be sought from the municipal
governments, that new buildings and newly installed central heating systems are
equipped with two-pipe heating systems that allow for individual household metering and
room temperature control.
57.  In summary, the proposed District Heating solutions present a win-win economic
opportunity: the package of fuel efficiency, least-cost operations, and net environmental
benefits makes the proposed project attractive from an economic, environmental, public
finance, and utility perspective.
Credit Line for Pollution Abatement (XRB Subloan Facility)
58.   The pollution abatement line of credit, described in detail in Annex 12, would
serve as a transitional financial mechanism to support enterprises that discharge into the
Xiaoqing River to reduce their pollution in a cost-effective way. The amount of
commercial or quota lending for environmental measures is very limited, because most
banks are still regulated by the planning commissions in forming their portfolio. The
pollution abatement line of credit, to be established under this project, would focus on
investments that demonstrate win-win opportunities where investments lead to efficiency
gains, which partly or fully offset the costs in abatement. Since the fund would deal with
heavy polluters, the marginal cleanup costs would be quite low for the initial pollution
abatement and the impact is estimated to be significant at an enterprise level. Since the
fund is currently limited to $10 million, the direct impact on the water quality of the
Xiaoqing River, however, would only be marginal. The subloan facility would be
supported by (a) the XRB water quality study; (b) the Basin time-bound Environmental
Action Plan; and (c) time-bound Action Plans for the three top-ranked polluters. These
commitments by SP to actually address pollution reduction would be expected to yield
high but not easily quantifiable returns.
C. AFFORDABILITY
59.   As part of the Government's policy to establish financially viable utilities, tariffs
for urban environmental services would be raised to a level where operational costs are
covered and a contribution made to future investments. Since these utilities provide



-177-                          ANNEX 15
essential services, the impact of these tariff increases on the poor was assessed, using for
reference the 1995 income of persons in "very poor households" as defined in Shandong
Province's 1995 Statistical Yearbook. For water and wastewater, the combined annual
cost does not exceed 3 percent at the suggested 1997 and 2000 tariff level. For district
heating, on the other hand, the share is 8.5 percent and drops to 6.5 percent in 2000.
While this is a considerable burden to poor households, the cost is comparable to current
expenditures for heating a coal stove. Even at drastically lower heating standards, annual
coal consumption is about 1.0 ton at an average cost of Y 488 per stove. If divided
among two income earners, the annual cost is Y 240 per person, compared to Y 230 per
person for district heating. Table 7 shows that the proposed project investments are
generally affordable.

D. PROJECT RISKS
60.   The Bank-supported components of the Shandong Environment Project form part
of a broader strategy to secure environmental improvements in the Xiaoqing River Basin
and enhanced well-being in Jinan and other project cities. Each component, if effectively
implemented, would make a local contribution to the regional objective of the River
Basin. However, to have a basinwide impact, the institutional innovations, the new
approaches to pollution management, and demonstrated technologies of each of the
components need to be replicated and complemented by sustained efforts across sectors
and throughout the Basin.
61.   Risks surrounding the project have therefore been divided into two broad
categories: Short/Medium-Term Project Risks and Long-Term Strategic Risks. The
former center around the immediate requirements of the project and the successful



-178 -                     ANNEX 15
implementation of its components. The latter center around the overall impact of the
project and relate to the sustainability of environmental improvements in the Xiaoqing
River Basin.
Short/Medium-Term Project Risks
62.   The principal risks directly associated with the implementation of the project and
the achievement of immediate project outputs can be grouped in environmental/
technical, financial and institutional issues (as summarized in Table 8 below).
Long-Term Strategic Risks
63.  Increased Environmental Pressures. Rapid economic and population growth in
the Xiaoqing River Basin area may simply exert too much pressure on available water
and land resources, and the emergent planning and environmental controls may not be
able to cope with this. Furthermore, the degree of ecological integrity and carrying
capacity of the environment are key parameters which would determine the level and
intensity of urban and rural development in the river basin, but many of these are
unknown at present. These long-term risks are summarized in Table 9 below. Increased
environmental pressures are caused by the following:
(a)   Inappropriate Resource Pricing. The underlying causes of a number of
environmental problems in the Xiaoqing River Basin area are in part a
product of underpricing resources and urban services, such as water and
heating. The transition toward sustainable resource use has begun with the
removal of subsidies on fuels, such as coal, and the introduction of tariffs
which ensure the financial viability of their providers. The next step
would require changes in resource pricing, which also reflect the
environmental effects of resource use, such as groundwater extraction and
water pollution.
(b)   Development Pressures from Industrialization. Other areas of potential
conflict include the push for industrial growth and need to protect the
environment. Given the high returns on investments in a fast-growing
economy, pollution reduction is competing for scarce capital with high
opportunity costs.  Only a systematic internalization environmental
externalities, as has started at a low level with the introduction of pollution
charges, would resolve this apparent conflict by transforming
environmental related expenditures into costs of doing business.
(c)   Development Pressures from  Urbanization.   Population growth
(including the floating population in the urban and peri-urban areas) would
lead to intensified competition for land and water while at the same time
increase pollution loads. A key risk here is that the pace of urban
development exceeds the financial and institutional capacity of Jinan
Municipal Government and other municipalities to expand urban
environmental service and pollution control measure fast enough.

improvement
64.   The investment components would, within their scope, mitigate some of these
risks by improving resources pricing and the provision of environmental services. The
technical assistance under the project would then play a key role in disseminating these
experiences and assist the Province to integrate them in the broader context of the river
basin. For example, the Long-Term Study for Xiaoqing River would provide planning
tools and data to anticipate and manage environmental risks.
Institutional Risks: Failure to Manage Environmental Pressures
65.      Increasing  environmental pressures necessitate  that institutions  must have  the
capacity to manage these pressures. Environmental management in the river basin is
marked by weak and fragmented institutions, lack of clear lines of authority and conflict
and lack of cooperation among institutions at all levels. To date, there has been a
reluctance to enforce regulations and make polluters pay. There would be an overriding
need for cooperation and coordination among institutions specifically responsible for
environmental protection and development control around the Xiaoqing River Basin. A
critical component of the success of the project is the enforcement by Shandong
Provincial Government (SPG), JMG, JEPB and other relevant EPBs of the existing and
enhanced environmental protection measures and regulations.
